الموضوع، وفي قوله : وإن كان بحث المتكلم إلخ، رد على العلامة التفتازاني حيث ذكر في التلويح أن قولنا من حيث كذا يجوز أن يتعلق بالبحث المذكور تضمنا في ضمن لفظ الموضوع، فحينئذ يجب أن يلاحظ الحيثية في البحث عن أحواله ولا يجب أن يكون له مدخل في العروض ووجه الرد أنه لا بد من المدخلية لئلا تصير أعراضاً غريبة، فتدبر . العلوم بحسب تمايز الموضوعات لا بالمحمولات، لأن المحمول لو جعل وجه التمايز بأن يكون البحث عن بعض الأحوال الذاتية علماً، وعن بعض آخر علماً آخر لم ينضبط أمر الاختلاف، والاتحاد ويكون كل علم علوماً جمةً ضرورة اشتماله على أنواع جمة من الأعراض كما ذكره في شرح المقاصد ، لأن أنواع الأعراض الذاتية إذا كانت داخلة تحت أمر جامع يحصل له الانضباط، بل لأن المحمولات صفات مطلوبة لذوات الموضوعات، فالأنسب أن تجعل جهة الوحدة قيد الموضوع على أن المقصود من اعتبار كل طائفة علماً على حدة، هو تسهيل أمر التعليم ولا نزاع في أن السهولة في جانب الموضوع أظهر منها في جانب المحمول فإن قلت : قد أجاب المحقق التفتازاني في التلويح الاعتراض عن السؤال المذكور بأن الموضوع لما كان عبارة عن المبحوث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية قيد بالحيثية على معنى أن البحث عن العوارض، يكون باعتبار الحيثية وبالنظر إليها أي يلاحظ في جميع المباحث هذا المعنى الكلي لا على معنى أن جميع العوارض المبحوث عنها لحوقها بهذه الحيثية إليه، وتلخيصه أن لفظ الموضوع يتضمن معنى فعلى البحث والعروض فالجار في قولهم موضوع الكلام المعلوم من حيث كذا متعلق بلفظ الموضوع باعتبار جزء معناه أعني البحث لا باعتبار الجزء الآخر أعني العروض حتى يلزم أن يكون للحيثية مدخل في عروض العوارض فلم لم يلتفت إليه الشارح قلت : لأن الحيثية إذا من تتمة الموضوع ولم يكن لها مدخل في عروض العوارض ، لم يصدق تعريف مطلق الموضوع على العلم المذكور، إذ لا يصدق حينئذ على الموضوع المقيد بالحيثية أنه يبحث في العلم عن أعراضه الذاتية إذ الأعراض على تقدير أن لا يكون للحيثية مدخل في العروض، ليست لذلك المقيد بل للمطلق، وهذا ظاهر وإن غفل عنه كثير من الناس.
The subject. And in his statement: 'And if the investigation of the theologian... etc.' is a refutation of al-Allama al-Taftazani where he mentioned in al-Talwih that our statement 'from such a consideration' may be attached to the mentioned investigation implicitly within the word subject, so then it is necessary to observe the consideration in the investigation of its states and it is not necessary for it to have a role in the accrual. The reason for the refutation is that the role is necessary to avoid them becoming extraneous accidents, so ponder. The sciences are distinguished according to the distinction of subjects, not by predicates, because if the predicate were made the basis of distinction such that investigating some essential states becomes a science and investigating others another science, the matter of difference and unity would not be regulated, and every science would be numerous sciences necessarily due to its inclusion of many types of accidents, as mentioned in Sharh al-Maqasid. Because the types of essential accidents, if they fall under a comprehensive matter, regulation is achieved, but rather because predicates are qualities sought for the essences of subjects, it is more fitting to make the aspect of unity a restriction of the subject, given that the purpose of considering each group as a separate science is to facilitate instruction, and there is no dispute that ease is more apparent on the side of the subject than on the side of the predicate. For if you say: The investigator al-Taftazani in al-Talwih has answered the objection to the mentioned question by saying that since the subject is an expression of what is investigated in the science regarding its essential accidents, it is restricted by the consideration, meaning that the investigation of accidents is by virtue of the consideration and in view of it, i.e., this universal meaning is observed in all investigations, not with the meaning that all the investigated accidents attach to it through this consideration. And its summary is that the word subject includes both the meanings of investigation and accrual, so the preposition in their statement 'the subject of kalam is the known from such a consideration' is attached to the word subject by virtue of one part of its meaning, namely investigation, not by virtue of the other part, namely accrual, so that it would necessitate that the consideration has a role in the accrual of accidents. So why did the commentator not pay attention to this? I say: Because if the consideration is part of the subject and has no role in the accrual of accidents, then the definition of absolute subject does not apply to the mentioned science, since then it would not apply to the subject restricted by the consideration that it is investigated in the science regarding its essential accidents, because the accidents, on the assumption that the consideration has no role in the accrual, are not for that restricted but for the absolute, and this is apparent even if many people are heedless of it.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of al-Taftazani's argument regarding the role of the consideration. Taftazani claimed that the consideration can be attached to the investigation (in the subject) without having a role in the accrual of accidents. The refutation first states that a role is necessary to avoid extraneous accidents. Then it discusses the basis of distinguishing sciences by subjects, not predicates, to ensure regulation. It includes a hypothetical objection and answer: Taftazani's own answer is that the word 'subject' includes both investigation and accrual, and the consideration is tied to investigation only. The commentator (the author of the sharh) replies that if the consideration is part of the subject but has no role in accrual, then the definition of the subject would not hold because the accidents would belong to the absolute subject, not the restricted one. This shows the necessity of the consideration's role in accrual.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The consideration's role is only in the investigation, not in the accrual, so the definition still holds.
Correction: The commentator shows that without a role in accrual, the definition of the subject as 'investigated regarding its essential accidents' does not apply to the restricted subject.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does Taftazani's answer fail?
A) Because the consideration must affect accrual for the definition to apply to the restricted subject.
B) Because Taftazani misunderstood the meaning of 'subject'.
C) Because the accidents are essential to the consideration.
D) Because the consideration is not part of the subject.

Key Takeaway
The consideration must affect both investigation and accrual; otherwise, the definition of the subject fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat definitions as merely formal tools, without ensuring they capture the reality they describe? When has an incomplete definition led you to an error in understanding a theological issue?
?
This passage warns that many are heedless of an apparent truth. Is there a settled belief you hold about Allah that you are afraid to examine too closely, lest it unravel? What would it cost you to re-evaluate it with the same rigor shown here?
?
If the consideration must affect both investigation and accrual, what does that demand of you when you study sacred knowledge? Are you willing to let the method of inquiry itself shape what you accept as true, or do you compartmentalize how you approach different subject matters?

The Subject of Kalam: The Essence of God
Definition of the subject of kalam as God's essence by al-Qadi al-Urmawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that according to al-Qadi al-Urmawi, the subject matter of the science of kalam is the essence of God Almighty.

Core Idea
The subject of kalam is the essence of God, from which all other discussions follow.

In this passage, al-Sharh introduces a key definition of the subject of kalam according to al-Qadi al-Urmawi. Understanding this definition helps clarify the scope of theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states that the subject of kalam is the essence of God, and attributes this view to al-Qadi al-Urmawi.
2
Reflect on the significance
Consider why defining the subject as the essence of God is important. It establishes that kalam's primary focus is on God Himself, not on creation or other topics.
This definition sets the foundation for all subsequent discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول - المرصد الأول : فيما يجب تقديمه في كل علم فلا يكون عرضاً ذاتياً له من تلك الحيثية، وإن كان بحث المتكلم عن قدرته تعالى لإثبات عقيدة دينية ( وقيل هو ( أي موضوع الكلام ) ذات الله تعالى ) والقائل بذلك هو القاضي الأرموي ( إذ يبحث فيه ) عن أعراضه الذاتية أعني ) عن صفاته ) الثبوتية والسلبية (و) عن ( أفعاله ) إما ( في الدنيا كحدوث العالم أي إحداثه ( و) إما (في الآخرة كالحشر) للأجساد ( و) عن (إحكامه فيهما كبعث الرسول ونصب الإمام ) في الدنيا من حيث أنهما واجبان عليه أم لا ) والثواب والعقاب في الآخرة من حيث أنهما يجبان عليه أم لا، ولا بد في هذه الأربعة من اعتبار قيد الوجوب أو عدمه، وإلا لكانت من قبيل الأفعال دون الأحكام).
And it is said that the subject of kalam is the essence of God Almighty, and the one who says that is al-Qadi al-Urmawi. For it investigates its essential accidents, namely, its positive and negative attributes, and its actions, either in this world like the creation of the world i.e. its origination, or in the afterlife like the gathering of bodies, and its judgments in both, like the sending of the messenger and the appointment of the imam in this world, from the perspective of whether they are obligatory upon Him or not, and reward and punishment in the afterlife from the perspective of whether they are obligatory upon Him or not. And it is necessary in these four to consider the qualification of obligation or its absence, otherwise they would be of the category of actions rather than judgments.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the subject matter of the science of kalam according to al-Qadi al-Urmawi. It is a definition passage. He states that the subject is the essence of God Almighty. Then he enumerates the accidental attributes (a'rad dhatiyya) that are investigated: (1) the positive and negative attributes (sifat thubutiyya wa salbiyya), (2) actions in this world such as the creation of the world, (3) actions in the afterlife such as the gathering of bodies, and (4) judgments (ahkam) such as sending prophets and appointing imams (in this world) and reward and punishment (in the afterlife). For each of these, the investigation concerns whether they are obligatory (wajib) upon God or not. He emphasizes that in these four categories, it is necessary to consider the condition of obligation (wujub) or its absence, otherwise they would be considered mere actions rather than judgments. This highlights that kalam is concerned with the normative aspects of God's actions and attributes.

Key Terms
موضوع الكلام
ذات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the subject of kalam is merely God's attributes or actions, not the essence itself.
Correction: Al-Qadi al-Urmawi explicitly states the subject is the essence, though it investigates the attributes and actions as accidental attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Qadi al-Urmawi, what is the subject of ilm al-kalam?
A) The actions of God
B) The essence of God Almighty
C) The attributes of God
D) The judgments of God

Key Takeaway
The subject of kalam is the essence of God Almighty, according to al-Qadi al-Urmawi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on God, do you focus more on His essence (who He is) or on His actions? How would shifting your attention to His essence transform your worship and awe?
?
The mutakallimun study God's power as a means to establish doctrine, but the subject is the essence. What is the 'essence' of your relationship with God—do you pursue deep knowledge of Him or are you content with surface-level arguments?
?
If the subject of kalam is God's essence, what does that demand of you in your own study of faith? Will you seek to know God Himself, or will you remain attached to secondary issues?

Accidental Attributes of the Subject of Kalam
Enumeration of the accidental attributes of God's essence investigated in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of accidental attributes that kalam investigates concerning the essence of God.

Core Idea
Kalam investigates four accidental attributes: attributes, actions in this world and the afterlife, and judgments with the condition of obligation.

Having defined the subject as God's essence, al-Sharh now lists the accidental attributes that are investigated. This enumeration shows the breadth of topics covered in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the four categories mentioned: positive/negative attributes, actions in this world, actions in the afterlife, and judgments in both worlds.
2
Identify the four categories
List the four categories: (1) positive and negative attributes, (2) actions in this world, (3) actions in the afterlife, (4) judgments in both worlds. Note that judgments are distinct from actions due to the condition of obligation.
3
Understand the condition of obligation
Pay attention to the last sentence: it is necessary to consider the condition of obligation (wujub) or its absence for these four. Otherwise, they would be mere actions rather than judgments.
This condition is crucial for understanding how kalam approaches moral and theological norms.

For each of these, the investigation concerns whether they are obligatory (wajib) upon God or not. What Is Kalam?

Objection: Kalam Discusses Substances and Accidents Not Tied to God
Al-Sharh raises the objection that kalam discusses topics like substances and accidents not tied to God, and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that kalam appears to investigate topics unrelated to God's essence, and the response that such topics must be problems of kalam, not mere preliminaries, because they are not self-evident.

Core Idea
In kalam, nothing is self-evident; even seemingly unrelated topics must be explained within the science.

This lesson addresses a key objection to defining kalam's subject as God's essence. Critics point out that kalam often discusses substances and accidents without explicit reference to God. How can such discussions be part of a science whose proper subject is God? The author of the Sharh presents the objection and offers a sophisticated response rooted in the distinction between problems (masa'il) and preliminaries (mabadi').

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythmic phrasing come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection starts with 'وفيه نظر من وجهين' but only the first aspect is covered.
2
Identify the objection
Find the part where the objector says that kalam investigates substances and accidents not from the perspective of their dependence on God. This is the challenge: if the subject is God, why discuss these?
The objector gives examples like 'two substances do not interpenetrate'.
3
Analyze the response's structure
The response argues that such discussions cannot be mere preliminaries (mabadi') because they are not self-evident. Then it presents two possibilities: either they are explained in kalam itself (making them problems that must relate to the subject) or in a higher science, which would have to be revealed and non-existent. Follow this logical chain.
Pay attention to the 'la yuqal' (it cannot be said) and 'fa innana naqul' (for we say) transitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيه نظر من وجهين الأول أنه قد يبحث فيه ) أي في الكلام ) عن غيرها ) أي عن غير ما ذكرت من الأعراض الذاتية لذاته تعالى ( كالجواهر والأعراض ) أي أحوالهما ( لا من حيث هي مستندة إليه تعالى ) حتى يمكن أن تدرج في البحث عن أعراضه الذاتية وذلك مثل قولهم الجوهران لا يتداخلان والأعراض لا تنتقل (لا يقال ذلك البحث إنما يورد في هذا العلم (على سبيل المبدئية ) لا على أنه من مسائله فلا يلزم أن يكون راجعاً إلى أحوال موضوعه ( لأنا نقول ليس ذلك ) البحث ) من الأمور البينة بذاتها حتى تكون من المبادي المطلقة ) المستغنية بالكلية عن البيان ( فلا بد من بيانه في علم فإن بين في هذا العلم فهو من مسائله)، فوجب أن يكون راجعاً إلى أحوال موضوعه، وليس كذلك كما عرفت ولا شبهة في جواز كون بعض مسائل علم مبدأ لمسائل أخرى منه ، إذا لم تتوقف الأولى على الأخرى، فتكون مسألة من جهة ومبدأ من جهة أخرى كما سيأتي ) أو في علم آخر) أي وإن بين في علم آخر ) كان ثمة علم أعلى منه ) أي من علم الكلام تبين فيه مباديه ( شرعي ( إذ لا يجوز أن تبين مباديه في علم أعلى غير شرعي، وإلا لاحتاج رئيس العلوم الشرعية على الإطلاق إلى علم أعلى غير شرعي ( وإنه ) أي ثبوت علم شرعي أعلى من علم الكلام ) باطل اتفاقاً)
And it has two aspects: First, that in kalam investigation is made of other than these, such as substances and accidents, i.e., their states, not from where they are dependent upon Him, such that they could be included in the investigation of His essential accidents. That is like their statement: two substances do not interpenetrate, and accidents do not transfer. It cannot be said that such investigation is introduced in this science as a preliminary (mabda') not as a problem (mas'ala), so it does not have to refer back to the states of its subject. For we say: that investigation is not among things evident by themselves such that it could be among the absolute preliminaries that are completely independent of explanation; therefore it must be explained in a science. If it is explained in this science, then it is among its problems, so it must refer back to the states of its subject, which is not the case as you know. There is no doubt about the permissibility of some problems of a science being preliminaries for other problems in the same science, if the former do not depend on the latter, so it is a problem from one aspect and a preliminary from another, as will come. Or if it is explained in another science, then there would be a higher science than kalam, a revealed one, for it is not permissible that its preliminaries be explained in a higher non-revealed science, otherwise the chief of the revealed sciences absolutely would need a higher non-revealed science. And the existence of a revealed science higher than kalam is false by agreement.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection (al-nazar) to the definition of the subject of kalam as God's essence. The objector says that kalam often discusses topics like substances and accidents (e.g., 'two substances do not interpenetrate') that are not intrinsically related to God’s essence. The response counters that such discussions cannot be mere preliminaries (mabadi') because they are not self-evident; they need explanation within a science. If explained in kalam, they become problems (masa'il) of kalam and must relate to its subject. Alternatively, if explained in another science, that science would be higher than kalam. But a revealed science higher than kalam does not exist, and a non-revealed higher science is not permissible. Therefore, either way, these topics must be part of kalam and must relate to its subject. However, they do not seem to relate to God's essence, so the objection stands, but the argument shows the complexity. The end of the passage asserts by agreement that there is no revealed science higher than kalam.

Key Terms
نظر
جواهر
أعراض
مبدئية
مسائل
علم أعلى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the response, why cannot the investigation of substances and accidents be considered mere preliminaries in kalam?
A) Because they are self-evident
B) Because they are not self-evident and need explanation
C) Because they are not related to God
D) Because they are problems of another science

Key Takeaway
Kalam's discussions of substances and accidents are not self-evident; they must be either problems within kalam that relate to God, or lead to a higher science that does not exist, thus justifying their inclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study topics in kalam that seem unrelated to Allah, do you feel they are irrelevant to your faith? How does this passage challenge you to see even 'unrelated' discussions as essential for understanding Allah's attributes? Are you willing to engage with such topics with patience and trust that they will deepen your knowledge of Him?
?
The passage insists that nothing in kalam is self-evident; everything requires explanation. In your own spiritual life, are there aspects of your beliefs that you have labeled 'self-evident' and never examined? What might you be neglecting by assuming they need no further thought? Will you commit to investigating even the basics of your faith more deeply?
?
The author mentions that a problem can serve as a preliminary for another problem. How can you apply this principle in your own learning—accepting that some difficult or seemingly irrelevant theological points may be prerequisites for greater understanding? Are you patient enough to master foundational issues without rushing to advanced topics?

The First Objection to the Subject of Kalam
The author presents the first objection that kalam discusses non-divine topics and provides a full response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the definition of kalam's subject matter and the author's detailed response defending that discussions of non-divine topics are legitimate within kalam.

Core Idea
Topics like atoms and accidents in kalam are either its own problems or belong to a higher shar'i science.

Before this passage, the author defined kalam as the science dealing with the essential attributes of God. But a critical objection arises: kalam often discusses topics like substances and accidents in themselves, seemingly unrelated to God. How can this be? The author addresses this objection with a careful argument that either these topics are part of kalam as problems or they belong to a higher science, which must be shar'i. This lesson walks through that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection that kalam discusses non-divine topics like substances and accidents, not as related to God but in themselves. Identify the claim: such topics seem outside the essential attributes of God.
The objector suggests these are brought in as principles, not problems.
2
Trace the author's response
Follow the author's reasoning: these topics are not self-evident, so they must be explained in some science. If explained in kalam, they become part of its problems; if in another science, that science must be higher and shar'i.
Note the condition that a higher science must be shar'i to preserve kalam's primacy.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the outcome: since no such higher shar'i science exists, the topics must be incorporated into kalam, justifying their presence.
The author also addresses a subsidiary objection about principles of a higher science being explained in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف الأول - المرصد الأول : فيما يجب تقديمه في كل أنهما يجبان عليه أم لا، ولا بد في هذه الأربعة من اعتبار قيد الوجوب أو عدمه، وإلا لكانت من قبيل الأفعال دون الأحكام) وفيه نظر من وجهين الأول أنه قد يبحث فيه عن غيرها (أي عن غير ما ذكرت من الأعراض الذاتية لذاته تعالى) كالجواهر والأعراض (أي أحوالهما) لا من حيث هي مستندة إليه تعالى حتى يمكن أن تدرج في البحث عن أعراضه الذاتية وذلك مثل قولهم الجوهران لا يتداخلان والأعراض لا تنتقل (لا يقال ذلك البحث إنما يورد في هذا العلم على سبيل المبدئية لا على أنه من مسائله فلا يلزم أن يكون راجعاً إلى أحوال موضوعه) لأنا نقول ليس ذلك البحث من الأمور البينة بذاتها حتى تكون من المبادي المطلقة المستغنية بالكلية عن البيان فلا بد من بيانه في علم فإن بين في هذا العلم فهو من مسائله، فوجب أن يكون راجعاً إلى أحوال موضوعه، وليس كذلك كما عرفت ولا شبهة في جواز كون بعض مسائل علم مبدأ لمسائل أخرى منه، إذا لم تتوقف الأولى على الأخرى، فتكون مسألة من جهة ومبدأ من جهة أخرى كما سيأتي (أو في علم آخر) أي وإن بين في علم آخر (كان ثمة علم أعلى منه) أي من علم الكلام تبين فيه مباديه (شرعي) إذ لا يجوز أن تبين مباديه في علم أعلى غير شرعي، وإلا لاحتاج رئيس العلوم الشرعية على الإطلاق إلى علم أعلى غير شرعي (وإنه) أي ثبوت علم شرعي أعلى من علم الكلام (باطل اتفاقاً)، ولقائل أن يقول: إن مبادئ العلم الأعلى قد تبين، وإن كان على قلة في العلم الأدنى فاللازم على ذلك التقدير ثبوت علم شرعي تبين فيه مبادئ الكلام أو احتياجه في مباديه إلى علم غير شرعي فإن سلم بطلان الثاني: فقد لا نسلم بطلان الأول، إلا أن يقال: ليس لنا علم شرعي يبين فيه ما نحن بصدده
The First Station - The First Observatory: On what must be presented in every [science]. Whether they are obligatory upon him or not, and it is necessary in these four to consider the condition of obligation or its absence, otherwise they would be among actions rather than judgments. And in it there is consideration from two aspects. The first: that it [i.e., kalam] may discuss other than it [i.e., other than the essential attributes of God Most High], such as substances and accidents (i.e., their states), not insofar as they are dependent on Him Most High, so that they could be included in the investigation of His essential attributes. That is like their saying: two substances do not interpenetrate, and accidents do not transfer. It cannot be said that such investigation is introduced in this science by way of being a principle, not as one of its problems, so it need not be referred to the states of its subject. For we say: that investigation is not among self-evident matters so as to be among absolute principles completely independent of explanation. So it must be explained in some science. If it is explained in this science, then it is among its problems. Thus it must be referred to the states of its subject, and it is not so as you have learned. And there is no doubt about the permissibility of some problems of a science being principles for other problems of it, if the former does not depend on the latter, so it is a problem from one aspect and a principle from another aspect, as will come. Or it [may be explained] in another science, i.e., if it is explained in another science, then there exists a science higher than it (i.e., than kalam) in which its principles are explained, [and that higher science must be] shar'i, for it is not permissible that its principles be explained in a higher non-shar'i science, otherwise the chief of all shar'i sciences would need a higher non-shar'i science. And that (i.e., the existence of a shar'i science higher than kalam) is false by agreement. And it may be said: the principles of the higher science have been explained, albeit rarely, in the lower science. Then the necessary consequence on that assumption is either the existence of a shar'i science in which the principles of kalam are explained, or its need in its principles for a non-shar'i science. If the falsity of the second is conceded, we may not concede the falsity of the first, unless it is said: we have no shar'i science that explains what we are dealing with.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument responding to an objection about the subject matter of kalam. The author begins by referring to a prior discussion about four things related to obligation, then states that there is a 'consideration from two aspects' (nazar min wajhayn). The first aspect is an objection: kalam sometimes discusses topics like substances and accidents, not insofar as they relate to God but in themselves (e.g., 'two substances do not interpenetrate'), which seems outside the essential attributes of God. The objector might say that such discussions are brought in as principles (mabadi') for the science, not as actual problems (masa'il), so they need not be about the subject. The author's response is that these topics are not self-evident principles; they require explanation. If they are explained within kalam, they become part of its problems and must relate to the subject's states. If they are explained in another science, that science must be higher than kalam and must be a shar'i (revealed) science, because otherwise kalam, the chief shar'i science, would depend on a non-shar'i science, which is unacceptable. The author then notes that no such higher shar'i science exists by agreement. A further objection is raised: the principles of the higher science might themselves be explained in the lower science (kalam), albeit rarely. In that case, either a shar'i science explaining kalam's principles exists, or kalam needs a non-shar'i science for its principles. If the latter is false (as argued), the former might still be denied by claiming there is no such shar'i science. Thus, the response shows that the objection is not fatal because the topics can be justified either as parts of kalam or as belonging to a higher shar'i science, which is absent, so they must be incorporated into kalam.

Misconception: Discussions about atoms and accidents in kalam are extraneous topics.
Correction: The author clarifies they are either principles of the science or belong to a higher science, which must be shar'i.

C) They are either principles of the science or belong to a higher science that must be shar'i.
D) They are only mentioned for the sake of refuting opponents.

Key Takeaway
Kalam's inclusion of topics like substance and accident is justified either as part of its own problems or as principles requiring a higher shar'i science, which does not exist.

This lesson shows that even principles must come from a higher shar'i source—are you willing to examine the foundations of your own beliefs with that same rigor?
?
The author insists that there is no shar'i science higher than kalam that explains its principles. How does that affect your confidence in the completeness of Islamic theology? Does it motivate you to study kalam more deeply, trusting that it is self-sufficient?
?
If you were to find that a central belief of yours depends on a principle that cannot be traced back to revelation, would you have the courage to reevaluate it? This passage warns against relying on non-shar'i principles—what would you do if faced with that discovery?

Absolute vs Particular Existence: Predication Rules
Defines absolute and particular existence and explains non-predication of the latter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between absolute and particular existence and why particular existence cannot be predicated by verbal agreement or derivation.

Core Idea
Particular existence is a unique concrete, not a universal predicate.

In this passage, the author distinguishes between two kinds of existence: the universal 'absolute existence' shared by all things and the unique 'particular existence' belonging to each thing individually. He then explains why the latter cannot be used as a predicate, addressing two forms of predication: by verbal agreement and by derivation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation.
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two types of existence.
Notice that absolute existence is common to all existents, while particular existence is unique to one thing.
Absolute existence is not a proper accident; particular existence is a true particular.
3
Understand the two reasons for non-predication.
The passage gives two arguments: by verbal agreement (a true particular cannot be a predicate) and by derivation (the owner of a particular accident is itself a particular).
Follow the commentary to see how the sharh explains each.
4
Reflect on the refuted objection.
The passage ends by refuting the claim that only derivation matters in proper accidents, showing that the owner of an accident is also a particular.
This clarifies why 'Zayd is the owner of this whiteness' is allowed but not 'Zayd is this whiteness'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجود المطلق مشترك بين الموجودات بأسرها، فلا يكون عرضاً ذاتياً لشيء منها، وأما الوجود الخاص بواحد منها فهو جزئي حقيقي، لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً. قوله : ( لا يحمل على شيء قطعاً) أما بالمواطأة فلما ذكر الشارح رحمه الله في تصانيفه أن الجزئي الحقيقي متأصل في الوجود لا ينتزع من شيء حتى يحمل عليه، وتفصيله في حواشينا على شرح الرسالة الشمسية، وأما بالاشتقاق فإن صاحب العرض الجزئي جزئي حقيقي لامتناع تشخص العارض بدون تشخص معروضه فاندفع ما قيل : إن المعتبر في حمل الأعراض الذاتية الحمل بالاشتقاق، ويجوز أن يقال زيد صاحب هذا البياض.
Absolute existence is common to all existents in their entirety, so it is not a proper accident for any of them. As for the particular existence of one of them, it is a true particular, not predicated of anything at all. His statement: 'not predicated of anything at all' – as for by verbal agreement (al-muwata'a), because the commentator (al-sharh), may God have mercy on him, mentioned in his works that the true particular is rooted in existence, not abstracted from anything such that it could be predicated of it; its detail is in our glosses on the commentary on al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. As for by derivation (al-ishtiqaq), the owner of a particular accident is a true particular due to the impossibility of the accidental being individuated without the individuation of its substrate. So it is refuted what was said: that what is considered in the predication of proper accidents is predication by derivation; and it is allowed to say 'Zayd is the owner of this whiteness.'
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines two types of existence: absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq) and particular existence (al-wujud al-khas). Absolute existence is common to all existents and is not a proper accident (arad dhati) for any of them because it is a universal. Particular existence, on the other hand, is a true particular (juz'i haqiqi) that cannot be predicated of anything. The commentary explains the impossibility of predication in two ways. First, by verbal agreement (al-muwata'a): a true particular is concrete and not abstracted from anything, so it cannot be a predicate. Second, by derivation (al-ishtiqaq): the owner of a particular accident is itself a true particular, so while one cannot say 'Zayd is this whiteness' (predication by verbal agreement), one can say 'Zayd is the owner of this whiteness' (predication by derivation). This refutes the objection that only derivation matters in proper accidents.

الوجود الخاص
عرض ذاتي
جزئي حقيقي
المواطأة
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That particular existence can be predicated just like universal existence, either by verbal agreement or derivation.
Correction: Particular existence is a true particular and cannot be predicated by verbal agreement; by derivation, only the form 'owner of the accident' is allowed, not direct predication.

Checkpoint
Why is particular existence not predicable by verbal agreement (al-muwata'a)?
A) Because it is an accident
B) Because it is a true particular that is not abstracted from anything
C) Because it is not a proper accident
D) Because it is not common

Key Takeaway
Absolute existence is universal and can be a predicate, but particular existence is a concrete particular and cannot be predicated directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If absolute existence is common to all things but cannot be a proper accident, how do you understand your relationship to God's existence? Are you satisfied with knowing Him only through His absolute attributes, or do you hunger for a personal encounter with the Particular?
?
The passage warns against directly predicating a particular accident of its owner; we must say 'possessor of.' Think of a time when you reduced someone to a single attribute (like their sin or achievement). Will you now intentionally see them as a particular person, not merely an attribute? What will change in how you treat them?

Is Kalam's Subject Existence as Such?
Presents and critiques the view that kalam's subject is existence as such.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversy over the subject matter of kalam, specifically the view that it is 'existence as such' and the two objections raised against it.

Core Idea
Kalam's subject is existence as such, but its method is Islamic law—not pure reason.

This passage presents and critiques the influential view that the subject of kalam is existence insofar as it exists. The author first clarifies what is meant by 'existence' and then distinguishes kalam from divine philosophy by its method. Finally, he raises two important objections that reveal the scope of kalam topics.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the meaning of 'inniyya'
Read the opening sentence where the author explains what is meant by the 'that-ness' (inniyya) of existence and non-existence. Note that he means only their predication of others.
This sets up the discussion by defining key terms.
2
Understand the proposed subject
Identify the view attributed to al-Ghazali: that kalam's subject is existence insofar as it exists, i.e., absolute existence. Then note how kalam is distinguished from divine philosophy by its method (Islamic law vs. pure reason).
The distinction is crucial for understanding the unique nature of kalam.
3
Examine the two objections
Read the two objections carefully. The first objection points out that kalam deals with non-existent entities and concepts like reflection and proof. The second objection notes that theologians deny mental existence, which is problematic for defining the subject as existence.
These objections challenge the comprehensiveness of the proposed definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانية الموجود الذي هو موضوع العلم الإلهي ولا نعني بأنيتهما سوى حملهما على غيرهما إيجاباً فتدبر ( وقيل هو أي موضوع الكلام (الموجود بما هو موجود) أي من حيث هو هو غير مقيد بشيء، والقائل به طائفة منهم حجة الإسلام ( ويمتاز ) الكلام ) عن الإلهي ( المشارك له في أن موضوعه أيضاً هو الموجود مطلقاً (باعتبار وهو أن البحث هاهنا ) أي في الكلام ) على قانون الإسلام) بخلاف البحث في الإلهي فإنه على قانون عقولهم وافق الإسلام أو خالفه ( وفيه أيضاً ) كالقول الأول ( نظر من وجهين الأول أنه قد يبحث فيه أي في الكلام (عن) أحوال ( المعدوم، والحال وعن أحوال ( أمور لا باعتبار أنها موجودة في الخارج أي يبحث فيه عن أحوال لأمور لا تتوقف تلك الأحوال على وجود تلك الأمور في الخارج سواء كانت موجودة فيه أم لا ( كالنظر والدليل) فيقال : مثلا النظر الصحيح يفيد العلم أم لا، والدليل وجه دلالته ،كذا وينقسم إلى كذا فإن هذه كلها مسائل كلامية كما ستعرفه لا وجود موضوعاتها في الخارج ( وأما الوجود في الذهن (فهم أي المتكلمون (لا يقولون به
The that-ness of existence (al-mawjud) which is the subject of the divine science, and we do not mean by their that-ness except predicating them of others affirmatively, so reflect. (And it is said: the subject of kalam is existence insofar as it exists (al-mawjud bi ma huwa mawjud), i.e., in itself, unrestricted by anything; the one who says this is a group, among them the Proof of Islam (al-Ghazali). (And kalam is distinguished from the divine science (al-ilahi) which shares with it in that its subject is also absolute existence, by a consideration: that inquiry here (i.e., in kalam) is according to the law of Islam (qanun al-Islam), contrary to inquiry in the divine science, which is according to the law of their intellects, whether it agrees with Islam or opposes it. (And in it also), like the first view, there is a critique from two aspects. The first: that it investigates in kalam the states of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and the state (al-hal), and the states of things not considered as existing externally, i.e., it investigates the states of things whose states do not depend on the existence of those things in the external world, whether they exist externally or not, like reflection (al-nazar) and the proof (al-dalil). So it is said, for example: Does correct reflection produce knowledge or not? And the proof: its aspect of indication, etc., and it is divided into such-and-such. For all these are kalam topics (masa'il kalamiyya), as you will know, and their subject matters have no external existence. (And as for existence in the mind (al-wujud al-dhihni), the theologians (al-mutakallimun) do not affirm it.)
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and critique of one opinion regarding the subject matter of kalam. First, the author clarifies the 'that-ness' (inniyya) of 'existence' (al-mawjud) and 'non-existence' (al-ma'dum), stating that by their 'that-ness' they mean only their predication of other things, not that they themselves are existent. Then he presents the view, attributed to a group including al-Ghazali, that the subject of kalam is 'existence insofar as it exists' (al-mawjud bi ma huwa mawjud), i.e., existence absolutely unrestricted. He then distinguishes kalam from divine philosophy (al-ilahiyyat) by noting that although both have the same subject (absolute existence), kalam's inquiry is conducted according to Islamic law (qanun al-Islam), whereas divine philosophy follows rational methods regardless of agreement with Islam. The author then raises two objections (nazar) to this view. The first objection is that kalam investigates topics such as the non-existent (al-ma'dum), the state (al-hal), and things like reflection (al-nazar) and proof (al-dalil) – all of which do not have external existence, so kalam's subject cannot be exhaustively defined as 'existence insofar as it exists.' The second objection is that the theologians (mutakallimun) generally deny mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni), which would be problematic if the subject were existence as such.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That kalam and divine philosophy are identical in subject and method.
Correction: The passage shows they share the subject (absolute existence) but differ methodologically: kalam follows Islamic law, divine philosophy follows reason alone.

Checkpoint

A) Kalam studies existence as such, while divine philosophy studies only God.
B) Kalam's inquiry follows Islamic law, while divine philosophy follows rational methods.
C) Kalam denies mental existence, while divine philosophy affirms it.
D) Kalam does not investigate non-existent things.

Key Takeaway
Kalam shares its subject with divine philosophy but differs in method; moreover, kalam's topics include non-existent and mental entities, challenging the definition of its subject as 'existence as such'.

The passage critiques a definition of kalam's subject. Have you ever critically examined the very subject of your own theological inquiries, or do you assume it is obvious? If you took the method of Islamic law as your primary guide, what would change about how you seek knowledge?

The Subject of Kalam: Existent qua Existent and the First Objection
Al-Iji defines the subject of kalam and presents the first objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Iji defines the subject of kalam as 'existent insofar as it is existent', how it is distinguished from divine philosophy, and the first objection that kalam also studies non-existent and mental things.

Core Idea
Kalam's subject is being in itself, but studied under Islamic law, not pure reason.

Al-Iji begins by stating a definition of the subject of kalam: the existent as such. He then distinguishes kalam from divine philosophy by noting that kalam operates under the law of Islam. However, an immediate objection arises: kalam seems to investigate non-existent and mental entities, which do not qualify as 'existent'. This passage presents that objection and begins a defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise style and the interplay between definition, distinction, and objection.
The passage includes a definition, a distinguishing characteristic, and the first of two objections.
2
Identify the definition and its nuance
Note that the definition is 'existent insofar as it is existent' (الموجود بما هو موجود), meaning being considered without any restriction. Identify the proponents, including al-Ghazali (Hujjat al-Islam).
3
Understand the distinction from divine philosophy
Both kalam and divine philosophy study existent absolutely, but kalam's research is 'according to the law of Islam' (على قانون الإسلام), while philosophy follows intellectual laws.
This distinction is crucial for defining the scope of kalam.
4
Examine the first objection
The objection states that kalam investigates states of the non-existent and entities like reflection and proof, which do not exist externally. This challenges the definition because these are not 'existent'.
The objection is presented as 'نظر من وجهين الأول' (a critical view from two aspects, the first).
5
Reflect on the rebuttal provided
The rebuttal clarifies that such investigations are about states of existent principles, and the existence of the subjects is not a condition for the investigation. The commentator also addresses mental existence.
The rebuttal is dense; focus on the main idea that the law of Islam governs the investigation, not the existence of the subjects.

موضعين، ثم لما كان إقحام لفظ الاعتبار في كلامه موهما بأن الواجب له مدخلية القيد في البحث، لا في العروض كما نقلته من التلويح وقد عرفت بطلانه فسر الشارح بما ذكره، ونص على أن المراد عدم مدخلية الوجود في إنما يرد إذا كان القائلون بأن موضوعه الموجود هم المتقدمون من المتكلمين النافين للوجود الذهني، وأما إذا كان بعضهم القائلين به فلا إلا أن يثبت بأدلة بطلانه وستعرف أنها غير تامة فتأمل . قوله: (أي .
And it is said: its meaning is the subject of kalam: 'existent insofar as it is existent', i.e., insofar as it is itself, not restricted by anything. Proponents of this include a group, among them Hujjat al-Islam. And kalam is distinguished from divine philosophy (al-ilahi), which shares with it the subject also being existent absolutely, by the consideration that research here (i.e., in kalam) is according to the law of Islam (qanun al-Islam), unlike research in divine philosophy which is according to the law of their intellects, whether it agrees with Islam or opposes it. And in it also, like the first opinion, there is a critical view from two aspects. The first: that it (i.e., in kalam) investigates the states of the non-existent and the state (al-hal), and the states of matters not considered as existent in the external world, i.e., it investigates the states of matters whose states do not depend on the existence of those matters in the external world, whether they exist in it or not, like reflection (al-nazar) and proof (al-dalil). For example, it is said: Does correct reflection yield knowledge or not? And the proof: its mode of indication, and so on, and it is divided into such-and-such. For all these are theological issues (masa'il kalamiyya) as you will know, whose subject matters have no existence in the external world. As for existence in the mind (al-wujud fi al-dhihn), they (i.e., the theologians) do not affirm it. His saying: 'And we do not mean, etc.' is a rebuttal of what might be objected that the non-existent and absolute existent are among mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) for which it is correct to say their quiddity (anniyya) is self-evident. The gist of the rebuttal is that by their quiddity is meant that their principle is existent and that they are predicated upon it, not that they exist in themselves. His saying: 'i.e., it investigates etc.' is a rebuttal, by this interpretation, of what might be objected to the apparent wording: that investigation of matters not considered as existent does not necessitate that those states are not states of existent matters, rather that their existence is not taken into account in the investigation, so the objection is not established. How then can it be considered? His saying: 'For all these are theological issues because they are means for establishing religious doctrines.' So the claim that the topics of reflection and proof are among the principles (mabadi') and the topics of the state and the non-existent are among the corollaries of the question of existence, to complete the intended purpose by addressing what opposes it, is an affectation. His saying: 'i.e., it investigates states etc.' Since what is investigated in a science are the states of the subject and its accidents, not its essence, the commentator (al-Sharih) imported the word 'states' in the author's speech in two places. Then, since inserting the word 'consideration' (al-i'tibar) in his speech might suggest that the condition must have a role in the investigation, not in the attribution, as I transmitted from al-Talwih and you have known its falsehood, the commentator interpreted it as he mentioned, and stressed that the intended meaning is that existence has no role in the attachment of those states, except that this aspect of consideration appears only if the distinguishing condition were existence, but it is not so; rather it is the condition that research be according to the law of Islam, so understand. His saying: 'As for existence in the mind, they (i.e., the theologians) do not affirm it.' This applies only if those who say its subject is the existent are the earlier theologians who denied mental existence. But if some of them affirm it, then it does not apply unless its falsehood is proven by proofs, and you will know that they are not complete, so reflect. His saying: 'i.e., the theologians, i.e., the majority of them, so it is not correct that absolute existent is its subject in their view; so it is not objected that many of the later ones affirm mental existence, so let absolute existence be its subject according to them, while Hujjat al-Islam, who affirms it, denies mental existence.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques a definition of the subject matter of ilm al-kalam, followed by an objection and its rebuttal. The author first reports a view: the subject of kalam is 'the existent insofar as it is existent' (al-mawjud bi ma huwa mawjud), meaning unrestricted being. Proponents include al-Ghazali (Hujjat al-Islam). To distinguish kalam from divine philosophy (al-ilahi), which also studies being absolutely, the author notes that kalam's research follows the law of Islam (qanun al-Islam), unlike philosophy which follows intellectual reasoning. Then he raises two objections (نظر من وجهين). The first objection: kalam investigates states (ahwal) of the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and of matters not considered as existent externally, such as reflection (al-nazar) and proof (al-dalil). These are theological issues whose subject matters have no external existence. The commentator (al-Sharh) then provides rebuttals (daf'). He explains that when the author says 'we do not mean' the quiddity (anniyya) of the non-existent or the absolute existent in themselves, he means that their principle is existent and they are predicated upon it. Similarly, when the author says 'it investigates states not considered as existent', he means that existence is not taken into account in the investigation, not that the states do not belong to existent things. The commentator also addresses the objection that these subjects might be considered mental existents (al-wujud fi al-dhihn). He notes that most early theologians deny mental existence, so including such subjects under 'existent insofar as it is existent' would not work for them. The commentator concludes by emphasizing that the distinguishing condition is the law of Islam, not existence, so the objection is not valid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that kalam's subject is limited to physical or external existents, ignoring mental and non-existent entities studied in kalam.
Correction: Kalam includes the study of mental and non-existent entities because they are related to existing principles and are analyzed under Islamic legal reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is kalam distinguished from divine philosophy?
A) Kalam studies only non-existent things.
B) Kalam's research is according to the law of Islam.
C) Kalam does not study existence at all.
D) Kalam rejects the use of reason.

Key Takeaway
Kalam studies existence itself, but always under the guidance of Islamic law, which allows it to also address non-existent and mental entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The subject of kalam is existence itself, but always under the law of Islam. Is your own life, day in and day out, truly lived under that law, or do you sometimes treat existence as a purely secular affair? What is one area where your actions suggest the latter?
?
Kalam studies even non-existent things like the state and mental entities. How real are Islamic concepts like intention, the afterlife, or the unseen in your daily consciousness? Have you ever caught yourself acting as if only the physical world matters?
?
This lesson shows that true kalam is bound by revelation, not free reason. When you think about Allah or existence, do you submit your thoughts to what Allah has revealed, or do you let your own reasoning be the final judge? What would change if you truly submitted in that area?

The Second Objection: Islamic Law Criterion Excludes Mistaken Theologians
Al-Sharh presents the second objection with sectarian examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of following Islamic law fails to distinguish kalam from non-kalam, as it would exclude the very people considered part of the discipline.

Core Idea
The Islamic law criterion backfires: it excludes mistaken theologians who are undeniably part of kalam.

In this passage, al-Sharh presents the second major objection to defining kalam's subject as research according to Islamic law. The objection shows that if we take 'law of Islam' to mean only true doctrine, then all false doctrines would be excluded, yet even heretical theologians are considered practitioners of kalam. This forces a refinement of the criterion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its logical structure
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow: the stated condition, the problem it creates, and the examples. Notice how the objection is constructed.
The objection uses a reductio: if law of Islam = true issues only, then false issues are excluded, but mistaken theologians are part of kalam.
2
Identify the examples and their significance
Spot the mentions of al-mujassima and al-Mu'tazila. Why are they used? They represent groups that are clearly mistaken (even declared disbelievers) yet their issues are still within kalam.
The examples reinforce that the criterion is too narrow.
3
Reflect on the implication for the definition
Consider: if the definition of kalam requires research according to Islamic law in the sense of true doctrine, then these groups would be excluded. But they are not. So the definition fails.
This sets up the need for an answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(الثاني قانون الإسلام ما هو الحق من المسائل الكلامية إذ المسائل الباطلة خارجة عن قانون الإسلام قطعاً فإن زعم القائل أن الكلام هو هذه المسائل الحقة فقط ورد عليه ما أشار إليه بقوله ( وبهذا القدر) أي بكون المسائل حقة على قانون الإسلام ) لا يتميز العلم ) أي علم الكلام عما ليس علم الكلام كيف وكل من صاحبي المسائل الحقة والباطلة ( يدعي ذلك ) أي كون مسائله حقة على قانون الإسلام (مع أن هذا الزعم منه باطل قطعاً، لأن ( المخطئ من أرباب علم (الكلام ومسائله من مسائل ،الكلام، كما أشير إليه بقوله ( وإن كفر) ذلك المخطئ كالمجسمة المصرحين بكونه تعالى جسماً، دون القائلين باتصافه بصفات الأجسام المتسترين بالبلكفة ( أو بدع) كالمعتزلة وقد يجاب عنه بأن المراد بكون البحث على قانون الإسلام أن تلك المسائل مأخوذة من الكتاب قوله: ( الثانى قانون إلخ حاصله أن هذا القيد وإن أفاد امتيازه عن الإلهي، لكنه مخل بالتعريف لأن قانون الإسلام إنما هو المسائل الحقة، فيلزم خروج مسائل المخطئ عن الكلام، وهو خلاف المقرر عندهم، وإن التزم مخالفة القوم يلزم عدم امتيازه عما ليس بكلام أعني مسائل المخطئ لأنه أيضاً يدعي أنها حقة، والجواب أن قانون الإسلام ما هو الحق، ولو بزعم الزاعم فيشمل مسائل المخطئ. قوله : (بالبلكفة) مأخوذ من بلا ،كيفية أي المستترين بنفي الكيفية حيث يقولون : أنه تعالى مستو على العرش لا كاستوائنا له وله وجه ويد لا كوجهنا ويدنا، وفي بعض النسخ بالكيفية فالباء للتعدية أي السائرين للكيفية. قوله : ( مأخوذة من الكتاب لعل مراده بالأخذ أن يحافظ في جميع تلك المباحث على القواعد الشرعية، ولا يخالف القطعية منها في اعتقاده فلا يرد أنه إذا لم يكن المخطئ مخالفاً للمسائل القطعية لا يصح تكفيره لأن من يكفره يعتقد أنه مخالف للقطعية، وإن لم يكن قوله : (مع أن هذا الزعم منه (باطل لا يقال : المراد بالحق أعم مما في نفس الأمر وعند الزاعم لأنا نقول : إذا أريد بقانون الإسلام ما هو الحق، وعمم الحق لكلا القسمين لم يحصل المطلوب وهو خروج الإلهي لأن صاحبه أيضاً يدعي حقيته.
The second: 'the law of Islam is that which is true from theological issues (al-masa'il al-kalamiyya), since false issues are definitely outside the law of Islam. For if the claimant says that kalam is only these true issues, he is refuted by what he himself indicated by his saying: 'And to this extent', i.e., by the issues being true according to the law of Islam, 'the science (i.e., the science of kalam) is not distinguished' from what is not the science of kalam. How so? For both the proponent of true issues and the proponent of false issues 'claim that', i.e., that his issues are true according to the law of Islam, while this claim of his is definitely false, because 'the mistaken one among the masters of the science of kalam and his issues are from the issues of kalam', as is indicated by his saying: 'even if that mistaken one disbelieves' like the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) who explicitly say that God is a body, unlike those who say He is described with attributes of bodies while concealing themselves by negating modality (al-balkafa), or innovators (bida') like the Mu'tazila. And it may be answered that what is meant by research being according to the law of Islam is that those issues are taken from the Book. His saying: 'The second: law, etc.' The gist of it is that this condition, although it distinguishes it from divine philosophy, yet it impairs the definition because the law of Islam is only the true issues, so it necessitates the exclusion of the mistaken person's issues from kalam, which contradicts what is established among them. And if one insists on opposing the school, it entails that it is not distinguished from what is not kalam, i.e., the mistaken person's issues, because he also claims they are true. And the answer is that the law of Islam is that which is true, even if by the claimant's claim, so it includes the mistaken person's issues. His saying: 'by al-balkafa' is derived from 'bila kayfiyya' (without modality), i.e., those who conceal themselves by negating modality, saying: He, exalted, is established on the Throne not like our establishment, and He has a face and hand not like our face and hand. In some copies it is 'bil-kayfiyya', so the ba' is for transitive, i.e., those who walk in modality. His saying: 'taken from the Book' perhaps he means by 'taking' that he preserves in all those investigations the legal principles, and does not violate the definitive ones among them in his belief, so it is not objected that if the mistaken one does not violate definitive issues, his disbelief is not valid, because the one who declares him disbeliever believes he is violating definitive issues, even if he is not. His saying: 'while this claim of his is false' it is not said: the intended meaning of truth is more general than what is in reality and what is according to the claimant's view, for we say: if what is meant by the law of Islam is that which is true, and truth is generalized to both kinds, the objective is not attained, namely the exclusion of divine philosophy, because its proponent also claims its truth.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage deals with the second objection to the definition of kalam's subject. The objection argues that if the distinguishing feature of kalam is that its research follows the law of Islam (qanun al-Islam), then since the law of Islam is only true issues (al-masa'il al-haqqa), false issues would be excluded from kalam. But even a mistaken theologian (al-mukhti') still belongs to the science of kalam, and his issues are considered kalam issues, as indicated by the author's words 'even if that mistaken one disbelieves'. Examples are anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) and innovators like the Mu'tazila. Therefore, the condition of Islamic law does not distinguish kalam from non-kalam, because both the true and the false claim their views are according to Islamic law. The commentator (al-Sharh) explains the objection: the condition helps distinguish from philosophy but undermines the definition because it would exclude mistaken views. He then offers an answer: the law of Islam is understood as what is true according to the claimant's claim, thus including mistaken views. The commentator further clarifies terms like 'balkafa' and discusses the possibility that 'taken from the Book' means adherence to legal principles. He concludes that if the condition is understood as truth in reality, it fails to exclude divine philosophy, whose proponents also claim truth.

قانون الإسلام
المسائل الحقة
المخطئ
المجسمة
المعتزلة
البلكفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The criterion of Islamic law cleanly separates true kalam from false doctrines.
Correction: This objection shows that even false sects claim their views are Islamic, so the criterion must include claims, not just objective truth.

Checkpoint
What does the second objection primarily demonstrate?
A) The law of Islam excludes all false doctrines from kalam.
B) Mistaken theologians are not part of kalam.
C) The criterion fails because both true and false claimants invoke Islamic law.
D) The Mujassima are not kalam.

Key Takeaway
The second objection reveals that the Islamic law criterion is too narrow because it would exclude the very practitioners of kalam who are in error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone whose beliefs differ from yours, do you ever dismiss them as not being 'real' Muslims or scholars? What does this passage teach you about the inclusion of even mistaken theologians in the field of kalam, and how might that change your attitude towards those you disagree with?
?
Are you quick to judge others' theology as outside the fold of Islam? How does the author's mention of the Mujassima and Mu'tazila as part of kalam challenge your instinct to exclude, and what criteria do you actually use to decide who is a true Muslim?
?
The law of Islam criterion seemed sound but failed upon scrutiny. Think of a personal 'criterion' you use to evaluate others' faith—perhaps based on actions, knowledge, or affiliation. Could that criterion also be flawed? What would it take for you to recognize that your standard might be too narrow?

Answer to the Objection: Broadening the Criterion to Include Claims
Al-Sharh provides the answer to the second objection and discusses key terms and a remaining problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator answers the second objection by reinterpreting 'law of Islam' to include the claimant's perspective.

Core Idea
The objection is solved by saying 'law of Islam' means what the claimant believes to be true, not only objective truth.

Having presented the objection, the commentator now offers an answer. He argues that the intended meaning of 'research according to Islamic law' is that the issues are taken from the Book and follow legal principles. Moreover, the law of Islam is understood as what is true according to the claimant's claim, thus including mistaken views. This response preserves the inclusion of heretical theologians while maintaining a link to Islamic sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer and note the key reinterpretation
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the answer: 'the law of Islam is that which is true, even if by the claimant's claim'. Understand how this broadens the criterion.
This reinterpretation allows mistaken theologians to remain within the definition.
2
Understand the term 'balkafa' and its context
The commentator explains 'balkafa' as derived from 'bila kayfiyya' (without modality). This clarifies a type of theologian who avoids anthropomorphism by negating modality.
This shows the commentator's attention to precise terminology.
3
Consider the concluding nuance about divine philosophy
At the end, the commentator points out that if 'truth' is generalized to both actual and claimed truth, the definition still fails to exclude divine philosophy, whose proponents also claim truth.
This suggests the answer is not fully satisfactory and further refinement is needed.

(الثاني قانون الإسلام ما هو الحق من المسائل الكلامية إذ المسائل الباطلة خارجة عن قانون الإسلام قطعاً فإن زعم القائل أن الكلام هو هذه المسائل الحقة فقط ورد عليه ما أشار إليه بقوله ( وبهذا القدر) أي بكون المسائل حقة على قانون الإسلام ) لا يتميز العلم ) أي علم الكلام عما ليس علم الكلام كيف وكل من صاحبي المسائل الحقة والباطلة ( يدعي ذلك ) أي كون مسائله حقة على قانون الإسلام (مع أن هذا الزعم منه باطل قطعاً، لأن ( المخطئ من أرباب علم (الكلام ومسائله من مسائل ،الكلام، كما أشير إليه بقوله ( وإن كفر) ذلك المخطئ كالمجسمة المصرحين بكونه تعالى جسماً، دون القائلين باتصافه بصفات الأجسام المتسترين بالبلكفة ( أو بدع) كالمعتزلة وقد يجاب عنه بأن المراد بكون البحث على قانون الإسلام أن تلك المسائل مأخوذة من الكتاب قوله: ( الثانى قانون إلخ حاصله أن هذا القيد وإن أفاد امتيازه عن الإلهي، لكنه مخل بالتعريف لأن قانون الإسلام إنما هو المسائل الحقة، فيلزم خروج مسائل المخطئ عن الكلام، وهو خلاف المقرر عندهم، وإن التزم مخالفة القوم يلزم عدم امتيازه عما ليس بكلام أعني مسائل المخطئ لأنه أيضاً يدعي أنها حقة، والجواب أن قانون الإسلام ما هو الحق، ولو بزعم الزاعم فيشمل مسائل المخطئ. قوله : (بالبلكفة) مأخوذ من بلا ،كيفية أي المستترين بنفي الكيفية حيث يقولون : أنه تعالى مستو على العرش لا كاستوائنا له وله وجه ويد لا كوجهنا ويدنا، وفي بعض النسخ بالكيفية فالباء للتعدية أي السائرين للكيفية. قوله : ( مأخوذة من الكتاب لعل مراده بالأخذ أن يحافظ في جميع تلك المباحث على القواعد الشرعية، ولا يخالف القطعية منها في اعتقاده فلا يرد أنه إذا لم يكن المخطئ مخالفاً للمسائل القطعية لا يصح تكفيره لأن من يكفره يعتقد أنه مخالف للقطعية، وإن لم يكن قوله : (مع أن هذا الزعم منه (باطل لا يقال : المراد بالحق أعم مما في نفس الأمر وعند الزاعم لأنا نقول : إذا أريد بقانون الإسلام ما هو الحق، وعمم الحق لكلا القسمين لم يحصل المطلوب وهو خروج الإلهي لأن صاحبه أيضاً يدعي حقيته.
The second: 'the law of Islam is that which is true from theological issues (al-masa'il al-kalamiyya), since false issues are definitely outside the law of Islam. For if the claimant says that kalam is only these true issues, he is refuted by what he himself indicated by his saying: 'And to this extent', i.e., by the issues being true according to the law of Islam, 'the science (i.e., the science of kalam) is not distinguished' from what is not the science of kalam. How so? For both the proponent of true issues and the proponent of false issues 'claim that', i.e., that his issues are true according to the law of Islam, while this claim of his is definitely false, because 'the mistaken one among the masters of the science of kalam and his issues are from the issues of kalam', as is indicated by his saying: 'even if that mistaken one disbelieves' like the anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) who explicitly say that God is a body, unlike those who say He is described with attributes of bodies while concealing themselves by negating modality (al-balkafa), or innovators (bida') like the Mu'tazila. And it may be answered that what is meant by research being according to the law of Islam is that those issues are taken from the Book. His saying: 'The second: law, etc.' The gist of it is that this condition, although it distinguishes it from divine philosophy, yet it impairs the definition because the law of Islam is only the true issues, so it necessitates the exclusion of the mistaken person's issues from kalam, which contradicts what is established among them. And if one insists on opposing the school, it entails that it is not distinguished from what is not kalam, i.e., the mistaken person's issues, because he also claims they are true. And the answer is that the law of Islam is that which is true, even if by the claimant's claim, so it includes the mistaken person's issues. His saying: 'by al-balkafa' is derived from 'bila kayfiyya' (without modality), i.e., those who conceal themselves by negating modality, saying: He, exalted, is established on the Throne not like our establishment, and He has a face and hand not like our face and hand. In some copies it is 'bil-kayfiyya', so the ba' is for transitive, i.e., those who walk in modality. His saying: 'taken from the Book' perhaps he means by 'taking' that he preserves in all those investigations the legal principles, and does not violate the definitive ones among them in his belief, so it is not objected that if the mistaken one does not violate definitive issues, his disbelief is not valid, because the one who declares him disbeliever believes he is violating definitive issues, even if he is not. His saying: 'while this claim of his is false' it is not said: the intended meaning of truth is more general than what is in reality and what is according to the claimant's view, for we say: if what is meant by the law of Islam is that which is true, and truth is generalized to both kinds, the objective is not attained, namely the exclusion of divine philosophy, because its proponent also claims its truth.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage deals with the second objection to the definition of kalam's subject. The objection argues that if the distinguishing feature of kalam is that its research follows the law of Islam (qanun al-Islam), then since the law of Islam is only true issues (al-masa'il al-haqqa), false issues would be excluded from kalam. But even a mistaken theologian (al-mukhti') still belongs to the science of kalam, and his issues are considered kalam issues, as indicated by the author's words 'even if that mistaken one disbelieves'. Examples are anthropomorphists (al-mujassima) and innovators like the Mu'tazila. Therefore, the condition of Islamic law does not distinguish kalam from non-kalam, because both the true and the false claim their views are according to Islamic law. The commentator (al-Sharh) explains the objection: the condition helps distinguish from philosophy but undermines the definition because it would exclude mistaken views. He then offers an answer: the law of Islam is understood as what is true according to the claimant's claim, thus including mistaken views. The commentator further clarifies terms like 'balkafa' and discusses the possibility that 'taken from the Book' means adherence to legal principles. He concludes that if the condition is understood as truth in reality, it fails to exclude divine philosophy, whose proponents also claim truth.

المسائل الحقة
البلكفة
المأخوذة من الكتاب
القطعية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reinterpreting 'law of Islam' as including the claimant's claim fully resolves the objection.
Correction: The commentator shows that if truth is generalized, the definition still fails to exclude divine philosophy, which also claims truth by its own criteria.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given, how does 'law of Islam' include mistaken theologians?
A) By referring to definitive legal principles.
B) By taking 'law of Islam' as what is true according to the claimant.
C) By requiring issues to be from the Book.
D) By excluding divine philosophy.

Key Takeaway
The objection is answered by understanding 'law of Islam' as truth from the claimant's perspective, but this raises further issues about excluding divine philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study kalam or any Islamic science, are you genuinely seeking objective truth, or are you more concerned with defending your own claims? How does the ambiguity in this definition challenge your intellectual honesty?
?
This lesson shows that even mistaken theologians are included in the field if they claim truth from Islamic sources. Are you willing to engage patiently with those you consider mistaken, or do you dismiss them without hearing their claims?
?
The remaining problem of divine philosophy exposes a flaw in the definition: claiming truth is not enough. In your own learning, where might you be conflating 'what I believe is true' with 'what is actually true' without adequate evidence from revelation or reason?

Objection and answer regarding the condition of the Law of Islam
Defense of the definition of kalam against the objection of excluding mistaken schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the definition of kalam against the objection that the condition 'according to the Law of Islam' mistakenly excludes mistaken schools.

Core Idea
The condition of Islam includes even mistaken views because it judges by the claimant's belief.

The definition of kalam includes the phrase 'according to the Law of Islam' to distinguish it from philosophy. But an objection arises: doesn't this exclude erroneous schools from kalam? The commentator argues that it actually includes them, because the Law of Islam considers truth even in the claimant's belief. This passage shows how precise theological definitions handle objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the first part where the objection is raised that the condition excludes mistaken schools, and the answer that the Law of Islam includes them by intent.
Notice the phrase 'ولو بزعم الزاعم' — this is key.
2
Understand the gloss on 'al-balkafah'
The commentator explains the term 'البلكفة' meaning 'bila kayfiyyah' (without modality). This is a reference to a school that avoids anthropomorphism by negating modality.
This gloss clarifies a technical term used in the definition.
3
Analyze the note on 'taken from the Book'
The commentator explains that 'taken from the Book' means preserving legal principles and not contradicting certainties in one's belief. This affects the ruling of takfir.
Consider how this connects to the broader issue of defining kalam.
4
Review the final objection and answer
Read the final objection that if 'right' is general, then divine philosophy is not excluded. The answer specifies that 'right' here means what is in accordance with Islam.
This reinforces the distinctiveness of the definition.

قوله: (الثاني قانون إلخ حاصله أن هذا القيد وإن أفاد امتيازه عن الإلهي، لكنه مخل بالتعريف لأن قانون الإسلام إنما هو المسائل الحقة، فيلزم خروج مسائل المخطئ عن الكلام، وهو خلاف المقرر عندهم، وإن التزم مخالفة القوم يلزم عدم امتيازه عما ليس بكلام أعني مسائل المخطئ لأنه أيضاً يدعي أنها حقة، والجواب أن قانون الإسلام ما هو الحق، ولو بزعم الزاعم فيشمل مسائل المخطئ.
قوله : (بالبلكفة) مأخوذ من بلا كيفية أي المستترين بنفي الكيفية حيث يقولون : أنه تعالى مستو على العرش لا كاستوائنا له وله وجه ويد لا كوجهنا ويدنا، وفي بعض النسخ بالكيفية فالباء للتعدية أي السائرين للكيفية.
قوله : ( مأخوذة من الكتاب لعل مراده بالأخذ أن يحافظ في جميع تلك المباحث على القواعد الشرعية، ولا يخالف القطعية منها في اعتقاده فلا يرد أنه إذا لم يكن المخطئ مخالفاً للمسائل القطعية لا يصح تكفيره لأن من يكفره يعتقد أنه مخالف للقطعية، وإن لم يكن
قوله : (مع أن هذا الزعم منه (باطل لا يقال : المراد بالحق أعم مما في نفس الأمر وعند الزاعم لأنا نقول : إذا أريد بقانون الإسلام ما هو الحق، وعمم الحق لكلا القسمين لم يحصل المطلوب وهو خروج الإلهي لأن صاحبه أيضاً يدعي حقيته.
His statement: (Second: the Law of Islam, etc.) Its summary: This condition, although it distinguishes it from the Divine Science, nevertheless it spoils the definition, because the Law of Islam is only the true issues. Thus it follows that the issues of the mistaken one are excluded from kalam, which is contrary to what is established among them. And if one insists on contradicting the people, then it follows that it is not distinguished from what is not kalam, i.e., the issues of the mistaken one, because he also claims they are true. And the answer is that the Law of Islam is what is true, even if by the claimant's belief, so it includes the issues of the mistaken one. His statement: (By al-Balkafah) It is derived from 'bila kayfiyyah' (without modality), meaning those who conceal themselves by negating modality, saying: He is established above the Throne not like our establishment, and He has a face and a hand not like our face and hand. In some copies it is 'bil-kayfiyyah', so the ba' is for transitivity, meaning those who follow modality. His statement: (Taken from the Book) Perhaps his intention by 'taking' is that he preserves in all those investigations the legal principles, and he does not contradict the certain ones among them in his belief. So it cannot be objected that if the mistaken one is not contradicting the certain issues, his takfir is invalid, because the one who declares him a disbeliever believes that he is contradicting certainties, even if he is not. His statement: (And this claim of his is false) It cannot be said: The intended meaning of 'right' is more general, including what is true in itself and what is true in the claimant's opinion. For we say: If by 'Law of Islam' is meant what is right, and 'right' is generalized to both categories, the desired result is not achieved, which is the exclusion of the Divine Science, because its practitioner also claims its truth.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections and answers defending the definition of kalam. The author's text included the condition 'according to the Law of Islam' to distinguish kalam from divine philosophy. The commentator presents an objection: this condition spoils the definition because it restricts kalam to true issues, thus excluding the issues of mistaken scholars (e.g., anthropomorphists) who are still considered part of kalam. The answer is that 'the Law of Islam' means 'what is true' even if only in the claimant's belief, so it includes mistaken views. The commentator then glosses the term 'al-balkafah' (bila kayfiyyah) used for those who avoid anthropomorphism by denying modality. Next, a note on 'taken from the Book' explains that the condition means preserving legal principles and not contradicting certainties in one's belief, so that the takfir of a mistaken scholar depends on the belief of the one declaring takfir. Finally, another objection: if 'right' is general, then divine philosophers also claim truth, so the distinction is lost. The answer is that the definition intends 'right' in the specific sense of what is in accordance with Islam, not general truth.
