The Five Investigations of the Second Observatory
The author enumerates the five investigations of the Second Observatory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five key investigations that constitute the Second Observatory and their role in establishing creedal beliefs.

Core Idea
The Second Observatory outlines five essential investigations for the theologian.

This lesson introduces the Second Observatory, which begins the actual content of kalam. It lists five investigations that a theologian must undertake, from defining knowledge to understanding theoretical reasoning. These investigations are the means to establish core creedal beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from the beginning to 'مسائل كلامية'
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the list of five investigations.
The passage uses a numbered sequence: أولاً, ثانياً, etc.
2
Identify each of the five investigations
Find each of the five items: the essence of knowledge, its division, necessary sciences, nazar, and the path of reasoning.
3
Understand the purpose of these investigations
The passage states that these five investigations are the means to establish creedal beliefs. Reflect on why knowledge, reasoning, and necessary sciences are prerequisite.
The author also says these investigations themselves are part of kalam, not borrowed from another science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني : في تعريف مطلق العلم من هاهنا في مقاصد علم الكلام، وما تقدم في المرصد الأول كان مقدمةً للشروع فيه، ولا بد للمتكلم من تحقيق ماهية العلم أولاً، ومن بيان انقسامه إلى ضروري ومكتسب ثانياً، ومن الإشارة إلى ثبوت العلوم الضرورية التي إليها المنتهى ثالثاً، ومن بيان أحوال النظر وإفادته للعلم رابعاً، ومن بيان الطريق الذي يقع فيه النظر ويوصل إلى المطلوب خامساً ، إذ بهذه المباحث يتوصل إلى إثبات العقائد، وإثبات مباحث أخرى تتوقف عليها العقائد وقد عرفت أنه قد جعل جميع ما يتوقف عليه إثبات العقائد من القضايا المكتسبة مقاصد في علمه كيلا يحتاج فيه إلى علم آخر، فالمباحث المذكورة في هذه المراصد الخمسة مسائل كلامية، وفي أبكار الأفكار تصريح بذلك حيث جعله مشتملاً على ثماني قواعد متضمنة لجميع مسائل الأصول . ( قوله : مسائل كلامية من وجه ومباد من وجه فلا ينافيه قوله: الموقف الأول في المقدمات لأن المراد منها ما يتوقف عليه جميع ما عداها إما شروعاً كما في المرصد الأول : أو ذاتاً كما في هذه المراصد الخمسة قوله : (تصريح إلخ) إذ لا يقال : إن الثمانية متضمنة لمسائل الأصول باعتبار تضمن خمسة منها، فلا بد أن تكون القواعد الثلاثة أيضاً متضمنة لمسائله والقول بأن بعض ما يذكر فيها مسائل دون بعض تحكم لم يقل به أحد، فيكون جميع ما يذكر فيها مسائل كلامية، فافهم فإنه زل فيه أقدام . قوله: (وما تقدم في المرصد الأول كان مقدمة الشروع فإن قلت: كلام الشارح ها هنا يناقض قول المصنف فيما سبق الموقف الأول في المقدمات، وفيه مراصد فإنه يدل على أن كل المراصد من المقدمة وقول الشارح يدل على أن المقدمة هي المرصد الأول فقط قلت : المرصد الأول مقدمة على الإطلاق، والمراصد الخمسة الباقية مقدمة من وجه، ومقاصد من وجه فمراد الشارح بالمقدمة على الإطلاق أعني مقدمة الشروع، ومراد المصنف أعم من ذلك فلا تناقض .
The Second Observatory: On the definition of absolute knowledge. From here onward are the objectives of the science of kalam, and what preceded in the First Observatory was an introduction to commencing it. It is necessary for the theologian to first investigate the essence of knowledge, second to explain its division into necessary and acquired, third to point to the establishment of the necessary sciences to which the end returns, fourth to explain the states of theoretical reasoning (al-nazar) and its yielding of knowledge, and fifth to explain the path in which theoretical reasoning occurs and leads to the desired end. For by these investigations one arrives at establishing the creedal beliefs and establishing other investigations upon which the creedal beliefs depend. And you have known that he has made all that which depends on establishing the creedal beliefs from acquired propositions objectives in his science so that one does not need another science for it. So the investigations mentioned in these five observatories are theological issues (masa'il kalamiyya). And in Abkar al-Afkar there is a clear statement of that as he made it containing eight bases (qawa'id) encompassing all the issues of the principles (usul). (His statement: issues theological in one respect and premises in another respect, so it does not contradict his statement: the First Standpoint is on the introductions, because what is meant by them is that upon which all others depend, either as commencement as in the First Observatory, or essentially as in these five observatories. His statement: (clear statement etc.) because it cannot be said that the eight bases encompass the issues of the principles by virtue of five of them encompassing them, so the three bases must also encompass its issues. And the statement that some of what is mentioned in them are issues and some are not is arbitrary, not said by anyone. So all that is mentioned in them are theological issues, so understand, for many have slipped here. His statement: (and what preceded in the First Observatory was an introduction to commencement. If you say: The commentator's statement here contradicts the author's statement previously: The First Standpoint is on the introductions, and it contains observatories, for it indicates that all the observatories are from the introduction, and the commentator's statement indicates that the introduction is only the First Observatory. I say: The First Observatory is an introduction absolutely, and the remaining five observatories are introductions in one respect and objectives in another respect. So what the commentator means by 'introduction absolutely' is the introduction to commencement, and what the author means is more general than that, so there is no contradiction.)
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a definition and structural outline. It begins by stating that the Second Observatory is about defining absolute knowledge and that everything after the First Observatory constitutes the actual objectives of kalam. It lists five sub-investigations: (1) the essence of knowledge, (2) its division into necessary and acquired, (3) the establishment of necessary sciences, (4) the states of theoretical reasoning and how it produces knowledge, and (5) the path of reasoning that leads to the goal. These five topics are the means to establish creedal beliefs. The author has made all prerequisite acquired propositions into objectives within this science to avoid reliance on another discipline. The passage then notes that these five observatories contain theological issues (masa'il kalamiyya) and cites Abkar al-Afkar as supporting this by stating that the work is built on eight bases (qawa'id) covering all principles. A comment clarifies that there is no contradiction between saying the observatories contain issues and saying they are premises: they are issues in one respect and premises in another. Finally, an objection is raised that the commentator seems to contradict the author: the author said the First Standpoint (al-mawqif al-awwal) is on introductions, implying all observatories are introductory, while the commentator said only the First Observatory is introductory. The answer resolves this by distinguishing between an absolute introduction (commencement) and a relative introduction (essential dependence). The First Observatory is an introduction absolutely, while the following five are both introductions (in one respect) and objectives (in another). Thus no contradiction exists.

Key Terms
العلم
ضروري
مكتسب
النظر
المراصد الخمسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five investigations of the Second Observatory are purely introductory and not theological issues.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that these investigations are theological issues (masa'il kalamiyya) and part of the objectives of kalam.

Checkpoint
How many investigations are listed in the Second Observatory?
A: Three
B: Four
C: Five
D: Six

Key Takeaway
The Second Observatory defines absolute knowledge and enumerates five investigations that are themselves theological issues essential for establishing creedal beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever personally investigated the nature of your own knowledge about Allah, or have you simply accepted what you were taught? This lesson calls you to make such investigations a part of your faith—are you willing to do that starting today?
?
The author insists that these five investigations are not optional extras but essential theological issues. Where in your life have you treated the foundations of your creed as secondary? What would change if you gave them the weight the author gives them?
?
If your belief in the truth of Islam does not rest on reasoning that you yourself have traversed, then upon what does it rest? This lesson forces you to confront whether your faith is built on necessary knowledge or on blind imitation—which is it, and what will you do about it?

Are the Observatories Issues or Premises?
The author clarifies the status of the observatories as both issues and premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the First Observatory as an absolute introduction and the remaining five as both introductions and objectives, and how this resolves an apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
The five observatories are both issues and premises—resolved by two kinds of introduction.

After establishing that the five observatories contain theological issues, the passage addresses a potential contradiction: the author previously said the First Standpoint is on introductions, yet here the observatories are called issues. A commentator's clarification shows that the First Observatory is an absolute introduction while the others are introductions in one respect and objectives in another.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from 'قوله: مسائل كلامية' to the end
This section contains the clarification and resolution of the contradiction.
Pay attention to the objection and the answer.
2
Identify the two kinds of introduction
The passage distinguishes between an introduction to commencement (mashru') and an essential introduction (dhati).
3
Understand why there is no contradiction
The First Observatory is introductory absolutely; the five are introductory in one respect (as prerequisites) and objectives in another (as issues).
The author's earlier statement is more general, covering both types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني : في تعريف مطلق العلم من هاهنا في مقاصد علم الكلام، وما تقدم في المرصد الأول كان مقدمةً للشروع فيه، ولا بد للمتكلم من تحقيق ماهية العلم أولاً، ومن بيان انقسامه إلى ضروري ومكتسب ثانياً، ومن الإشارة إلى ثبوت العلوم الضرورية التي إليها المنتهى ثالثاً، ومن بيان أحوال النظر وإفادته للعلم رابعاً، ومن بيان الطريق الذي يقع فيه النظر ويوصل إلى المطلوب خامساً ، إذ بهذه المباحث يتوصل إلى إثبات العقائد، وإثبات مباحث أخرى تتوقف عليها العقائد وقد عرفت أنه قد جعل جميع ما يتوقف عليه إثبات العقائد من القضايا المكتسبة مقاصد في علمه كيلا يحتاج فيه إلى علم آخر، فالمباحث المذكورة في هذه المراصد الخمسة مسائل كلامية، وفي أبكار الأفكار تصريح بذلك حيث جعله مشتملاً على ثماني قواعد متضمنة لجميع مسائل الأصول . ( قوله : مسائل كلامية من وجه ومباد من وجه فلا ينافيه قوله: الموقف الأول في المقدمات لأن المراد منها ما يتوقف عليه جميع ما عداها إما شروعاً كما في المرصد الأول : أو ذاتاً كما في هذه المراصد الخمسة قوله : (تصريح إلخ) إذ لا يقال : إن الثمانية متضمنة لمسائل الأصول باعتبار تضمن خمسة منها، فلا بد أن تكون القواعد الثلاثة أيضاً متضمنة لمسائله والقول بأن بعض ما يذكر فيها مسائل دون بعض تحكم لم يقل به أحد، فيكون جميع ما يذكر فيها مسائل كلامية، فافهم فإنه زل فيه أقدام . قوله: (وما تقدم في المرصد الأول كان مقدمة الشروع فإن قلت: كلام الشارح ها هنا يناقض قول المصنف فيما سبق الموقف الأول في المقدمات، وفيه مراصد فإنه يدل على أن كل المراصد من المقدمة وقول الشارح يدل على أن المقدمة هي المرصد الأول فقط قلت : المرصد الأول مقدمة على الإطلاق، والمراصد الخمسة الباقية مقدمة من وجه، ومقاصد من وجه فمراد الشارح بالمقدمة على الإطلاق أعني مقدمة الشروع، ومراد المصنف أعم من ذلك فلا تناقض .
The Second Observatory: On the definition of absolute knowledge. From here onward are the objectives of the science of kalam, and what preceded in the First Observatory was an introduction to commencing it. It is necessary for the theologian to first investigate the essence of knowledge, second to explain its division into necessary and acquired, third to point to the establishment of the necessary sciences to which the end returns, fourth to explain the states of theoretical reasoning (al-nazar) and its yielding of knowledge, and fifth to explain the path in which theoretical reasoning occurs and leads to the desired end. For by these investigations one arrives at establishing the creedal beliefs and establishing other investigations upon which the creedal beliefs depend. And you have known that he has made all that which depends on establishing the creedal beliefs from acquired propositions objectives in his science so that one does not need another science for it. So the investigations mentioned in these five observatories are theological issues (masa'il kalamiyya). And in Abkar al-Afkar there is a clear statement of that as he made it containing eight bases (qawa'id) encompassing all the issues of the principles (usul). (His statement: issues theological in one respect and premises in another respect, so it does not contradict his statement: the First Standpoint is on the introductions, because what is meant by them is that upon which all others depend, either as commencement as in the First Observatory, or essentially as in these five observatories. His statement: (clear statement etc.) because it cannot be said that the eight bases encompass the issues of the principles by virtue of five of them encompassing them, so the three bases must also encompass its issues. And the statement that some of what is mentioned in them are issues and some are not is arbitrary, not said by anyone. So all that is mentioned in them are theological issues, so understand, for many have slipped here. His statement: (and what preceded in the First Observatory was an introduction to commencement. If you say: The commentator's statement here contradicts the author's statement previously: The First Standpoint is on the introductions, and it contains observatories, for it indicates that all the observatories are from the introduction, and the commentator's statement indicates that the introduction is only the First Observatory. I say: The First Observatory is an introduction absolutely, and the remaining five observatories are introductions in one respect and objectives in another respect. So what the commentator means by 'introduction absolutely' is the introduction to commencement, and what the author means is more general than that, so there is no contradiction.)
Source pages p. 68

Commentary

It begins by stating that the Second Observatory is about defining absolute knowledge and that everything after the First Observatory constitutes the actual objectives of kalam. It lists five sub-investigations: (1) the essence of knowledge, (2) its division into necessary and acquired, (3) the establishment of necessary sciences, (4) the states of theoretical reasoning and how it produces knowledge, and (5) the path of reasoning that leads to the goal. These five topics are the means to establish creedal beliefs. The author has made all prerequisite acquired propositions into objectives within this science to avoid reliance on another discipline. The passage then notes that these five observatories contain theological issues (masa'il kalamiyya) and cites Abkar al-Afkar as supporting this by stating that the work is built on eight bases (qawa'id) covering all principles. A comment clarifies that there is no contradiction between saying the observatories contain issues and saying they are premises: they are issues in one respect and premises in another. Finally, an objection is raised that the commentator seems to contradict the author: the author said the First Standpoint (al-mawqif al-awwal) is on introductions, implying all observatories are introductory, while the commentator said only the First Observatory is introductory. The First Observatory is an introduction absolutely, while the following five are both introductions (in one respect) and objectives (in another). Thus no contradiction exists.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The five observatories are purely introductory like the First Observatory, and not theological issues in their own right.
Correction: The passage argues that they are issues in one respect (as objectives) and premises in another (as prerequisites), distinguishing them from the absolute introduction of the First Observatory.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the apparent contradiction between the author and himself regarding the number of introductory observatories?
A: He says both are wrong.
B: He distinguishes between absolute introduction (commencement) and relative introduction (essential dependence).
C: He claims the author changed his mind.
D: He ignores the contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The five observatories are both theological issues and premises, and the First Observatory is an absolute introduction, resolving an apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your pursuit of knowledge, do you treat certain subjects as merely introductory steps to be rushed through, forgetting that they also carry their own weight as issues worthy of deep contemplation? How can you change your approach today?
?
When you encounter an apparent contradiction in what you learn, do you dismiss one source entirely, or do you pause to consider that the contradiction may be resolved by more careful distinctions? Will you commit to seeking resolution before rejecting?
?
The lesson warns against making arbitrary distinctions between what counts as an issue and what doesn't. Where in your own thinking about deen have you made such arbitrary boundaries, dividing topics into 'important' and 'unimportant' based on convenience rather than truth? Will you revisit those judgments?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
6 min
Al-Razi's First Proof That Absolute Knowledge Is Necessary
Al-Razi's first proof that absolute knowledge is necessary, with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi argues that absolute knowledge is necessary (daruri) by demonstrating that it is a part of the necessary knowledge of one's own existence.

Core Idea
Absolute knowledge is necessary because it is part of something everyone knows necessarily.

Al-Razi, one of the great theologians, held that our concept of knowledge itself is not acquired through reflection but is innate. In this passage, he offers his first proof: everyone necessarily knows that they exist, and absolute knowledge is part of that particular knowledge. Since the part is prior to the whole, absolute knowledge must be even more fundamental. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Don't worry about understanding every detail yet; focus on the overall flow: the claim, the proof, and the commentary.
Notice that the passage begins by stating there are three schools, then gives al-Razi's first proof.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify key terms like 'absolute knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq), 'necessary' (ḍarūrī), 'particular knowledge' (al-ʿilm al-khāṣṣ), 'part' (juzʾ), and 'whole' (kull). Understanding these will help you grasp the proof's structure.
3
Work through the core syllogism
The proof can be broken into a syllogism. The major premise: whatever is prior to the necessary is necessary. The minor premise: absolute knowledge is prior to the necessary knowledge of existence. Conclusion: absolute knowledge is necessary. Try to see how the minor premise is justified by the part-whole relationship.
The minor premise relies on the idea that absolute is intrinsic to restricted, so absolute knowledge is a part of the particular knowledge of existence.
4
Consider the commentary's clarifications
Read the commentary to understand potential objections and why the proof holds. The commentator explains that the 'two reasons' could be proofs or prompts (tanbīhān), and addresses disputes about the self-evidence of existence knowledge.
The commentary also formalizes the proof as a syllogism and answers an objection about self-evidence.

المرصد الثاني - في تعريف مطلق العلم
( وفيه ) أي في العلم المطلق ( ثلاثة مذاهب ) المذهب ( الأول أنه ضروري ) أي تصور ماهيته بالكنه ) واختاره الإمام الرازي لوجهين) الوجه ( الأول أن علم كل أحد بوجوده ) أي بأنه موجود ) ضروري ) أي حاصل له بلا اكتساب ونظر ( وهذا علم خاص ) متعلق بمعلوم خاص هو وجوده ) والعلم المطلق جزء منه ) لأن المطلق ذاتي للمقيد ( والعلم بالجزء سابق على العلم بالكل) فإذا حصل العلم الخاص الذي هو كل لكل أحد بالضرورة، كان العلم المطلق الذي هو جزؤه سابقاً عليه ( والسابق على الضروري أولى أن يكون ضرورياً فالعلم المطلق ضروري ) وهو
قوله: (لوجهين) أي لدليلين بناء على أن الحكم ببداهة البديهي يجوز أن يكون نظرياً كيفية حصوله ابتداء لقلة العمل في حصوله، واختلاطه بالعلوم الكثيرة، أو تنبيهين بناءً على أن يكون الحكم بالبداهة أيضاً بديهياً لكن كثرة المناقشة فيهما تتأتى عن كونهما للغفلة عن تنبيهين بديهي.
قوله: (أي بأنه موجود إلخ) لم يحمله على ما هو الظاهر من أن تصور كل أحد لوجوده لأن الإمام قرره في كتبه بالعلم بأنه موجود لا لأنه يرد عليه إنه إن أريد به الوجود الخاص فلا نسلم أن تصوره بديهي، وإن أريد به الوجود المقيد بالإضافة، فهو فرع ثبوت الوجود المطلق، ولا نسلم ثبوته ولأن في بداهة تصوره مناقشة سواء أريد به الوجود الخاص، أو المقيد حيث أنكر جمهور المتكلمين الوجود الخاص، وأثبتوا الخصص، والشيخ أنكر الخصص لنفيه الوجود المطلق، ثم لا يخفى أن العلوم الجزئية الضرورية من التصورات والتصديقات كثيرة، فتخصيص الاستدلال ببداهة هذا العلم الجزئي أعني العلم بوجوده بناء على أنه أسبق العلوم الضرورية، على ما قالوا : توجيه الوجه الأول على قانون الاستدلال أن يقال : العلم المطلق سابق على العلم الضروري والسابق على الضروري ضروري أما الكبرى فظاهرة، وأما الصغرى فلانه جزء من العلم الضروري بأنه موجود، والجزء سابق على الكل أما جزئيته منه فلانه مطلق، وذاك مقيد والمطلق جزء المقيد، وأما ضروريته فلحصوله من غير كسب وكل ما شأنه هذا فهو ضروري . يختلف فيها، ويشك أنها يستفاد منها ذلك وإلا يكون إلغازاً مجتنباً عنه في هذا المقام لا يفيد اللزوم كما لا يخفى .
قوله : ( لوجهين الأول إن علم كل أحد إلخ) بداهة العلم بشيء لا يستلزم العلم البديهي ببداهته ولذا استدل عليها وأما ما يقال أن ماهيته إذا حصلت للنفس بلا كسب والتفتت النفس إليها عرفت بمجرد التفاتها إليها أنها بغير كسب فتكون بداهة كل بديهي غنية عن الاحتجاج عليها، وكذا كسبية كل كسبي فجوابه أنه قد يحصل في النفس صورة، ولا يلتفت إلى كيفية حصولها، فإذا تطاولت المدة، وتكثرت الصور وتوجهت النفس إليها، فربما التبست عليها كيفية حصول بعضها، فاحتاجت إلى الاستدلال على أن الوجهين جاز أن يكونا تنبيهين.
The Second Observation — On the Definition of Absolute Knowledge. (Within it), i.e., concerning absolute knowledge, (there are three schools). The (first school) is that it is necessary (ḍarūrī), i.e., the conception of its quiddity in its essence. And Imam al-Razi chose it for two reasons. The (first reason) is that the knowledge of every person of his existence, i.e., that he exists, is necessary, i.e., obtained without acquisition and reflection. And this is a particular knowledge pertaining to a particular object, namely his existence. And absolute knowledge is a part of it because the absolute is intrinsic (dhātī) to the restricted (al-muqayyad). And knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole. So when the particular knowledge which is a whole is obtained by everyone necessarily, absolute knowledge which is its part is prior to it. And that which is prior to the necessary is more deserving of being necessary, so absolute knowledge is necessary. It is... His statement: 'for two reasons' meaning two proofs, based on the idea that judging the self-evidence of the self-evident may be theoretical (naẓarī) in terms of its initial occurrence due to lack of practice in its occurrence and its intermingling with many sciences, or two prompts (tanbīhān) based on the idea that the judgment of self-evidence is also self-evident, but the frequency of discussion about them may stem from heedlessness of two self-evident prompts. His statement: 'i.e., that he exists etc.' He did not interpret it as the apparent meaning, that is, the conception by everyone of his existence, because the Imam established in his books that it is knowledge that he exists, not for the reason that it is objected that if particular existence is intended, we do not concede that its conception is self-evident; and if restricted existence through relation is intended, then it is a branch of the establishment of absolute existence, and we do not concede its establishment; and because there is dispute about the self-evidence of its conception, whether particular or restricted existence is intended, since the majority of theologians denied particular existence and affirmed particularization (takhṣīṣ), and the Shaykh denied particularization due to his denial of absolute existence. Then it is not hidden that the particular necessary sciences among concepts and assents are many, so specifying the inference by the self-evidence of this particular science, namely the knowledge of his existence, on the basis that it is the most prior of the necessary sciences, as they said: The direction of the first reason according to the rule of inference is that one says: absolute knowledge is prior to the necessary knowledge, and what is prior to the necessary is necessary. As for the major premise, it is apparent. As for the minor premise, it is because it is a part of the necessary knowledge that he exists, and the part is prior to the whole. As for its being a part of it, it is because it is absolute and that is restricted, and the absolute is part of the restricted. As for its necessity, it is because it is obtained without acquisition, and everything that is such is necessary. There is disagreement about it and doubt that it benefits from that, otherwise it would be an enigma avoided in this context and does not benefit the implication, as is not hidden. His statement: 'for two reasons, the first that the knowledge of everyone etc.' The self-evidence of knowledge of a thing does not entail self-evident knowledge of its self-evidence, hence it is inferred. And as for what is said that if its quiddity is obtained for the soul without acquisition and the soul attends to it, it knows by virtue of its attending to it that it is without acquisition, so the self-evidence of every self-evident is independent of evidence for it, and similarly the acquisition of every acquired, the answer is that an image may be obtained in the soul, and it does not attend to how it was obtained. Then when time passes, and images multiply, and the soul attends to them, perhaps the manner of obtaining some of them becomes confused for it, so it needs to infer that the two reasons may be two prompts.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof (al-wajh al-awwal) for the school that absolute knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), attributed to Imam al-Razi. The passage opens by stating that there are three schools regarding absolute knowledge, and the first school holds it to be necessary. Al-Razi is said to have chosen this position and supported it with two proofs. The first proof is as follows: everyone has necessary knowledge of their own existence (i.e., that they exist). This is a particular knowledge ('ilm khāṣṣ). Absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq) is a part of this particular knowledge because the absolute is intrinsic (dhātī) to any restricted (muqayyad) instance; here, absolute knowledge is the abstract concept, while the knowledge of one's own existence is a restricted instance. Since knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole, and the particular knowledge (the whole in this context) is obtained necessarily, absolute knowledge (the part) is prior to it. Whatever is prior to the necessary is itself more deserving of being necessary. Hence, absolute knowledge is necessary. The commentary then clarifies that 'for two reasons' could mean two proofs or two prompts (tanbīhān) for awakening attention. The commentator explains that the self-evidence of a self-evident proposition may itself be acquired theoretically, or it may be self-evident but obscured by inattention. He then addresses potential objections: some philosophers deny that knowledge of existence is self-evident, distinguishing between particular existence and absolute existence, and disputes about the nature of existence. The commentator also notes that many particular sciences are necessary, and specifying the inference by the knowledge of existence is based on its priority. He then formalizes the first proof as a syllogism: major premise— whatever is prior to the necessary is necessary; minor premise— absolute knowledge is prior to the necessary knowledge (of existence); conclusion— absolute knowledge is necessary. The minor premise is supported by the part-whole relationship. Finally, the commentary addresses the objection that self-evidence does not entail explicit awareness of self-evidence: one may have a concept without noticing how it was acquired, so even self-evident things might need prompting. Therefore, the 'two reasons' could be prompts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That absolute knowledge is acquired through reasoning (naẓar) because it is an abstract concept, and thus not necessary.
Correction: The proof shows that absolute knowledge is a part of the necessary knowledge of existence, and since the part precedes the whole, it is even more fundamental and therefore necessary, not acquired.

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of al-Razi's first proof that absolute knowledge is necessary?
A) Absolute knowledge is self-evident because everyone knows they exist.
B) Absolute knowledge is a part of the necessary knowledge of existence, and the part precedes the whole, so it is prior and thus necessary.
C) Absolute knowledge is acquired because it requires reflection on existence.
D) Absolute knowledge is necessary because it is the same as the knowledge of existence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof establishes that absolute knowledge is necessary because it is a part of the necessary knowledge of one's own existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to know you exist. That knowledge is given to you without effort. But do you live as if the knowledge of Allah, of which your own existence is only a part, is just as certain? Or do you allow doubts to creep in, as if that absolute knowledge were not necessary? What one concrete step will you take today to treat the knowledge of Allah as the most certain reality in your life?
?
Al-Razi argues that even necessary truths can be overlooked due to inattention. What necessary truth about your Lord—His oneness, His power, His knowledge—have you been heedless of? How will you intentionally remind yourself of it daily?
?
If absolute knowledge is a necessary part of your own self-knowledge, then denying or doubting it is like denying a part of yourself. Are you living in full acknowledgment that Allah's knowledge encompasses everything, or do you act secretly as if some matters are hidden from Him? What will you do this week to align your actions with that certainty?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
6 min
Al-Razi's First Proof: Absolute Knowledge is Necessary
Al-Iji presents the first opinion and al-Razi's first proof with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first proof that absolute knowledge is necessary, including its logical structure and the role of particular knowledge of existence.

Core Idea
Absolute knowledge is a part of the necessary particular knowledge of one's own existence.

Before we can evaluate competing theories of knowledge, we must understand how the first opinion—that absolute knowledge is immediate—was defended. Imam al-Razi offered two proofs, and the first one relies on the self-evident knowledge each person has of their own existence. This lesson unpacks that proof and the commentary that clarifies its logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Focus on the structure: opinion, proof, and the commentary that explains the proof.
Note that the passage uses parenthetical insertions to clarify terms.
2
Identify the key components of the proof
Find the statements that establish: (1) every person knows their existence necessarily, (2) this is particular knowledge, (3) absolute knowledge is a part of it, (4) part precedes whole, (5) what precedes the necessary is necessary.
These five steps form the logical chain.
3
Reflect on the commentary
The commentary explains that al-Razi's two reasons may be proofs or reminders. Consider why the self-evidence of the premise might itself need demonstration.
This sets up the response in the next lesson.

أحد التصورات اتباعاً للمتقدمين، وإن اعتبار تصور النسبة في التصديق من تدقيقات المتأخرين، فقد ركب شططاً . قوله : ( والجواب عنه أن الضروري حصول علم إلخ ) فإن قلت : سيجيء في بحث العلم من الإلهيات أن العلم بالعلم لا يتوقف إلا على الالتفات ولهذا ظن أن العلم بالشيء عين العلم بالعلم، وحينئذ يندفع هذا الجواب، ويحتاج إلى جواب الشارح قلت : المذكور فيما سيأتي أن من علم شيئاً أمكنه أن يعلم أنه عالم به، لا أن أنه يعلمه وإليه أشار في مباحث العلم من موقف الأعراض فجواب الشارح أظهر. قوله : ( والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق إنما لم يقل أحد تصوراته اتباعاً للمتقدمين فإن اعتبار تصور النسبة في التصديق من تدقيقات المتأخرين .
The second observatory - on the definition of absolute knowledge. The first section on knowledge and its divisions, the second on speculation (al-naẓar) and what pertains to it, the third on the methods leading to theoretical objectives. (And in it), i.e., concerning absolute knowledge, (there are three doctrines). The (first) doctrine (is that it is necessary/immediate (ḍarūrī)), i.e., the conceptualization of its quiddity (māhiyya) in its essence. And Imam al-Razi chose it for two reasons. The (first reason) (is that every person's knowledge of his existence), i.e., that he exists, (is necessary), i.e., obtained without acquisition and speculation. (And this is particular knowledge) belonging to a particular object which is his existence. (And absolute knowledge is a part of it) because the absolute is essential to the restricted. (And knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole). So when the particular knowledge, which is a whole for everyone, is obtained necessarily, then absolute knowledge, which is its part, precedes it. (And what precedes the necessary is more worthy of being necessary, so absolute knowledge is necessary). His saying: (for two reasons), i.e., two proofs, based on the fact that the judgment of the self-evidence of the self-evident may be theoretical in the manner of its initial occurrence due to the lack of practice in its occurrence and its intermingling with many knowledges, or two reminders based on the fact that the judgment of self-evidence is also self-evident, but the frequency of discussion about them results from the fact that they are [due to] heedlessness of two reminders. Self-evident. His saying: (i.e., that he exists, etc.) He did not interpret it as apparent, namely that everyone's conceptualization of his existence, because the Imam established in his books that it is knowledge that he exists, not because it is objected against him that if what is meant is particular existence, we do not concede that its conceptualization is self-evident, and if what is meant is existence restricted by addition, then it is a branch of the affirmation of absolute existence, and we do not concede its affirmation. And because there is discussion about the self-evidence of its conceptualization whether particular existence is meant or restricted, since the majority of theologians (mutakallimūn) denied particular existence and affirmed individuation (al-khuṣūṣ), and the Shaykh denied individuation due to his denial of absolute existence. Then it is not hidden that the particular necessary sciences from conceptualizations and assents are many, so specifying the inference by the self-evidence of this particular science, namely the knowledge of his existence, based on the fact that it is the most prior of the necessary sciences, according to what they said: The direction of the first proof according to the law of inference is that it be said: Absolute knowledge precedes necessary knowledge, and what precedes the necessary is necessary. As for the major premise, it is apparent. As for the minor, because it is a part of the necessary knowledge that he exists, and the part precedes the whole. As for its being a part of it, because it is absolute and that is restricted, and the absolute is part of the restricted. As for its necessity, because it is obtained without acquisition, and everything whose state is this is necessary. It is disputed and doubted that it is derived from that; otherwise it would be an enigma avoided in this context, not beneficial for implication, as is not hidden. His saying: (for two reasons, the first that every person's knowledge, etc.) The self-evidence of knowledge of something does not entail self-evident knowledge of its self-evidence; therefore it is inferred. As for what is said that if its quiddity occurs to the soul without acquisition and the soul attends to it, it knows merely by its attending to it that it is without acquisition, so the self-evidence of every self-evident is independent of proof, and likewise the acquiredness of every acquired, then the answer is that an image may occur in the soul without attending to how it occurred. When time passes and images multiply and the soul turns to them, perhaps the manner of occurrence of some is confused, so it needs inference. So the two reasons could be two reminders. (And the answer) to it (is that the necessary is the occurrence of particular knowledge) related to his existence; for this knowledge is obtained by everyone without speculation. (And it), i.e., the occurrence of that particular knowledge, (is not its conceptualization nor does it entail it) because often particular knowledges occur to us concerning specific objects, and we do not conceptualize those knowledges despite their occurring to us; rather, we need a renewed attention to conceptualize them. So their occurrence is neither identical to their conceptualization nor entailing it. And if that particular knowledge related to his existence is not conceptualized, (then the conceptualization of absolute knowledge does not follow at all), let alone that its conceptualization be necessary. And it may be answered about it also that it is only complete if knowledge is essential to what is under it and if some of its individuals are conceptually grasped by essence self-evidently, and both are denied. (It should not be said:) We do not restrict ourselves to what was mentioned; rather we say: Everyone knows necessarily that he exists, and also knows similarly that he knows that, and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent, and it is also [immediate], so its conceptualization preceding the self-evident assent is more worthy of being self-evident. We [say] self-evident: His saying: so its occurrence is not identical to its conceptualization. And what is said that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary according to what the author stated in [the chapter on] accidents, so knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, and absolute knowledge is part of it, so the approximation is complete, it is nothing because the meaning of that is that knowledge of the occurrence of knowledge after attention is necessary, not that its conceptualization is necessary such that the necessity of the absolute follows. Because his saying: (and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) He only said one of the two conceptualizations without specification; it may be made subject by saying knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, or predicate by saying everyone knows this knowledge necessarily. And by this it appears that it is not permissible to say one of the three conceptualizations. Whoever says that he only did not say one of the conceptualizations following the predecessors, and that considering the conceptualization of the relation in the assent is from the refinements of the later scholars, has certainly committed excess. His saying: (And the answer to it that the necessary is the occurrence of knowledge, etc.) For if you say: It will come in the discussion of knowledge from the divine matters that knowledge of knowledge depends only on attention; therefore it is thought that knowledge of a thing is identical to knowledge of knowledge. Then this answer is refuted, and the commentator's answer is needed. I say: What is mentioned in what will come is that whoever knows a thing can know that he knows it, not that knowledge depends only on attention. Moreover, the opponent's doubt is about the affirmation of God's knowledge, so we are not obliged to concede it. The truth is that what is mentioned in the divine matters, even if it is as mentioned, yet the apparent is that whoever knows a thing and attends to it knows merely by his attention that he knows it. And to this he pointed in the discussions of knowledge from the Station of Accidents, so the commentator's answer is more evident. His saying: (And knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) He only did not say one of its three conceptualizations following the predecessors, for considering the conceptualization of the relation in the assent is among the refinements of the later scholars.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the definition of absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq). The author first presents the first of three opinions: that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge is necessary/immediate (ḍarūrī), meaning it is acquired without effort or speculation. This opinion is attributed to Imam al-Razi, who offers two proofs. The first proof is explained in detail: everyone has immediate knowledge of their own existence; this is a particular instance of knowledge (ʿilm khāṣṣ); absolute knowledge is a part of it (since the absolute is a genus for the restricted); knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole; therefore, since the particular knowledge is necessary, the absolute knowledge that is its part must be even more prior and thus also necessary.

The commentary then elaborates on this proof, noting potential objections and clarifications. For example, it discusses whether the knowledge of existence is really self-evident and addresses the possibility that the proof could be seen as a reminder rather than a strict demonstration.

Next, the passage presents a response (al-jawāb) to al-Razi's proof. The response distinguishes between the mere occurrence (ḥuṣūl) of particular knowledge and its conceptualization (taṣawwur). It argues that while the knowledge of existence occurs to everyone without speculation, this occurrence does not entail that one has conceptualized that knowledge. Often we have particular knowledges without being aware of them as concepts; we need to attend to them separately to conceptualize them. Therefore, the occurrence of particular knowledge does not imply the conceptualization of absolute knowledge, let alone its necessity.

Then a further objection (lā yuqāl) is raised: everyone not only knows they exist but also knows that they know this. This second-order knowledge (that one knows) is an immediate assent (taṣdīq), and knowledge is one of its two conceptualizations. Since the conceptualization of knowledge precedes the assent, it should be even more immediate. The response to this objection clarifies that the necessary knowledge of knowledge after attention is only the judgment that one knows, not the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge. The conceptualization itself may still be acquired. Further commentary discusses the distinction between the subject and predicate in the judgment and refutes the idea that three conceptualizations are involved, following the method of the later scholars.

Finally, additional commentary responds to a potential objection from the discussion of knowledge in the divine attributes, asserting that the later explanation of knowledge of knowledge requiring only attention does not undermine the present argument, as the issue there is different.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because everyone knows they exist, the universal concept of knowledge is also immediately known to everyone.
Correction: The proof distinguishes between having particular knowledge and having the universal concept; the universal is a part of the particular and is not automatically conceptualized.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of al-Razi's first proof?
A) Absolute knowledge is self-evident to everyone.
B) Every person's knowledge of their own existence is necessary.
C) The universal is known only through speculation.
D) Particular knowledge is a part of absolute knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first proof attempts to show that absolute knowledge is necessary by arguing it is a part of the necessary particular knowledge of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your knowledge of your own existence is necessary and immediate, yet the commentary warns that you may be heedless of how it arises. Are you actively mindful of the very foundations of your certainty, or do you take them for granted? What would change if you paused daily to reflect on the source of your most basic knowledge?
?
Al-Razi argues that absolute knowledge—the universal concept of knowledge—is a part of your immediate self-awareness. Do you live as though your particular experiences are rooted in universal truths, or do you get lost in the particulars? How might reorienting your perspective toward the universal affect your worship and understanding of Allah?
?
The proof shows that even self-evident truths may need a reminder to be recognized. What essential truth about your faith or existence has become obscured by heedlessness, and what specific reminder from this lesson could bring it back into clarity? Commit to one action today to reawaken that truth in your heart.

What Is Kalam?
advanced
7 min
Response: Occurrence vs. Conceptualization of Knowledge
Al-Iji presents the response to al-Razi's first proof and a further objection with resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical response to al-Razi's first proof: that the mere occurrence of a particular knowledge does not entail its conceptualization, and thus does not prove the necessity of the universal concept.

Core Idea
Occurrence of knowledge is not the same as conceptualization; the former can happen without the latter.

Al-Razi's proof seemed compelling, but a decisive response shows a hidden assumption: that having a particular knowledge automatically means one has conceptualized it. The response argues that often we have knowledges without being consciously aware of them as concepts. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the subsequent objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the response, which begins with 'والجواب عنه'. Focus on the key claim: 'the necessary is the occurrence of particular knowledge, not its conceptualization'.
Notice that the response does not deny that knowledge of existence occurs necessarily.
2
Understand the occurrence vs. conceptualization distinction
The response argues that knowledge can occur in the soul without being conceptualized. We need to attend to it separately to form a concept. So occurrence does not imply conceptualization.
Think of examples: you have knowledge of many things without actively thinking about the concept of that knowledge.
3
Examine the objection and resolution
Read the objection starting with 'لا يقال' and its answer. Consider how second-order knowledge (knowing that one knows) is handled.
The distinction between subject and predicate in the judgment is crucial.

The second observatory - on the definition of absolute knowledge. The first section on knowledge and its divisions, the second on speculation (al-naẓar) and what pertains to it, the third on the methods leading to theoretical objectives. (And in it), i.e., concerning absolute knowledge, (there are three doctrines). The (first) doctrine (is that it is necessary/immediate (ḍarūrī)), i.e., the conceptualization of its quiddity (māhiyya) in its essence. And Imam al-Razi chose it for two reasons. The (first reason) (is that every person's knowledge of his existence), i.e., that he exists, (is necessary), i.e., obtained without acquisition and speculation. (And this is particular knowledge) belonging to a particular object which is his existence. (And absolute knowledge is a part of it) because the absolute is essential to the restricted. (And knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole). So when the particular knowledge, which is a whole for everyone, is obtained necessarily, then absolute knowledge, which is its part, precedes it. (And what precedes the necessary is more worthy of being necessary, so absolute knowledge is necessary). His saying: (for two reasons), i.e., two proofs, based on the fact that the judgment of the self-evidence of the self-evident may be theoretical in the manner of its initial occurrence due to the lack of practice in its occurrence and its intermingling with many knowledges, or two reminders based on the fact that the judgment of self-evidence is also self-evident, but the frequency of discussion about them results from the fact that they are [due to] heedlessness of two reminders. Self-evident. His saying: (i.e., that he exists, etc.) He did not interpret it as apparent, namely that everyone's conceptualization of his existence, because the Imam established in his books that it is knowledge that he exists, not because it is objected against him that if what is meant is particular existence, we do not concede that its conceptualization is self-evident, and if what is meant is existence restricted by addition, then it is a branch of the affirmation of absolute existence, and we do not concede its affirmation. And because there is discussion about the self-evidence of its conceptualization whether particular existence is meant or restricted, since the majority of theologians (mutakallimūn) denied particular existence and affirmed individuation (al-khuṣūṣ), and the Shaykh denied individuation due to his denial of absolute existence. Then it is not hidden that the particular necessary sciences from conceptualizations and assents are many, so specifying the inference by the self-evidence of this particular science, namely the knowledge of his existence, based on the fact that it is the most prior of the necessary sciences, according to what they said: The direction of the first proof according to the law of inference is that it be said: Absolute knowledge precedes necessary knowledge, and what precedes the necessary is necessary. As for the major premise, it is apparent. As for the minor, because it is a part of the necessary knowledge that he exists, and the part precedes the whole. As for its being a part of it, because it is absolute and that is restricted, and the absolute is part of the restricted. As for its necessity, because it is obtained without acquisition, and everything whose state is this is necessary. It is disputed and doubted that it is derived from that; otherwise it would be an enigma avoided in this context, not beneficial for implication, as is not hidden. His saying: (for two reasons, the first that every person's knowledge, etc.) The self-evidence of knowledge of something does not entail self-evident knowledge of its self-evidence; therefore it is inferred. As for what is said that if its quiddity occurs to the soul without acquisition and the soul attends to it, it knows merely by its attending to it that it is without acquisition, so the self-evidence of every self-evident is independent of proof, and likewise the acquiredness of every acquired, then the answer is that an image may occur in the soul without attending to how it occurred. When time passes and images multiply and the soul turns to them, perhaps the manner of occurrence of some is confused, so it needs inference. So the two reasons could be two reminders. (And the answer) to it (is that the necessary is the occurrence of particular knowledge) related to his existence; for this knowledge is obtained by everyone without speculation. (And it), i.e., the occurrence of that particular knowledge, (is not its conceptualization nor does it entail it) because often particular knowledges occur to us concerning specific objects, and we do not conceptualize those knowledges despite their occurring to us; rather, we need a renewed attention to conceptualize them. So their occurrence is neither identical to their conceptualization nor entailing it. And if that particular knowledge related to his existence is not conceptualized, (then the conceptualization of absolute knowledge does not follow at all), let alone that its conceptualization be necessary. And it may be answered about it also that it is only complete if knowledge is essential to what is under it and if some of its individuals are conceptually grasped by essence self-evidently, and both are denied. (It should not be said:) We do not restrict ourselves to what was mentioned; rather we say: Everyone knows necessarily that he exists, and also knows similarly that he knows that, and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent, and it is also [immediate], so its conceptualization preceding the self-evident assent is more worthy of being self-evident. We [say] self-evident: His saying: so its occurrence is not identical to its conceptualization. And what is said that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary according to what the author stated in [the chapter on] accidents, so knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, and absolute knowledge is part of it, so the approximation is complete, it is nothing because the meaning of that is that knowledge of the occurrence of knowledge after attention is necessary, not that its conceptualization is necessary such that the necessity of the absolute follows. Because his saying: (and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) He only said one of the two conceptualizations without specification; it may be made subject by saying knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, or predicate by saying everyone knows this knowledge necessarily. And by this it appears that it is not permissible to say one of the three conceptualizations. Whoever says that he only did not say one of the conceptualizations following the predecessors, and that considering the conceptualization of the relation in the assent is from the refinements of the later scholars, has certainly committed excess. His saying: (And the answer to it that the necessary is the occurrence of knowledge, etc.) For if you say: It will come in the discussion of knowledge from the divine matters that knowledge of knowledge depends only on attention; therefore it is thought that knowledge of a thing is identical to knowledge of knowledge. Then this answer is refuted, and the commentator's answer is needed. I say: What is mentioned in what will come is that whoever knows a thing can know that he knows it, not that knowledge depends only on attention. Moreover, the opponent's doubt is about the affirmation of God's knowledge, so we are not obliged to concede it. The truth is that what is mentioned in the divine matters, even if it is as mentioned, yet the apparent is that whoever knows a thing and attends to it knows merely by his attention that he knows it. And to this he pointed in the discussions of knowledge from the Station of Accidents, so the commentator's answer is more evident. His saying: (And knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) He only did not say one of its three conceptualizations following the predecessors, for considering the conceptualization of the relation in the assent is among the refinements of the later scholars.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about the definition of absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq). The author first presents the first of three opinions: that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge is necessary/immediate (ḍarūrī), meaning it is acquired without effort or speculation. This opinion is attributed to Imam al-Razi, who offers two proofs. The first proof is explained in detail: everyone has immediate knowledge of their own existence; this is a particular instance of knowledge (ʿilm khāṣṣ); absolute knowledge is a part of it (since the absolute is a genus for the restricted); knowledge of the part precedes knowledge of the whole; therefore, since the particular knowledge is necessary, the absolute knowledge that is its part must be even more prior and thus also necessary.

The commentary then elaborates on this proof, noting potential objections and clarifications. For example, it discusses whether the knowledge of existence is really self-evident and addresses the possibility that the proof could be seen as a reminder rather than a strict demonstration.

Next, the passage presents a response (al-jawāb) to al-Razi's proof. The response distinguishes between the mere occurrence (ḥuṣūl) of particular knowledge and its conceptualization (taṣawwur). It argues that while the knowledge of existence occurs to everyone without speculation, this occurrence does not entail that one has conceptualized that knowledge. Often we have particular knowledges without being aware of them as concepts; we need to attend to them separately to conceptualize them. Therefore, the occurrence of particular knowledge does not imply the conceptualization of absolute knowledge, let alone its necessity.

Then a further objection (lā yuqāl) is raised: everyone not only knows they exist but also knows that they know this. This second-order knowledge (that one knows) is an immediate assent (taṣdīq), and knowledge is one of its two conceptualizations. Since the conceptualization of knowledge precedes the assent, it should be even more immediate. The response to this objection clarifies that the necessary knowledge of knowledge after attention is only the judgment that one knows, not the conceptualization of the essence of knowledge. The conceptualization itself may still be acquired. Further commentary discusses the distinction between the subject and predicate in the judgment and refutes the idea that three conceptualizations are involved, following the method of the later scholars.

Finally, additional commentary responds to a potential objection from the discussion of knowledge in the divine attributes, asserting that the later explanation of knowledge of knowledge requiring only attention does not undermine the present argument, as the issue there is different.

Key Terms
علم جزئي
تصور
حصول
تصديق
بديهي
التفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That having a particular knowledge automatically means one has conceptualized that knowledge.
Correction: The response clarifies that occurrence is not conceptualization; one can have knowledge without forming a concept of it.

Checkpoint
According to the response, why does the necessary occurrence of particular knowledge of existence not prove that absolute knowledge is necessary?
A) Because absolute knowledge is not part of that particular knowledge.
B) Because the occurrence of knowledge is not the same as its conceptualization.
C) Because the particular knowledge is not necessary.
D) Because the proof is only for the second opinion.

Key Takeaway
The critical response shows that necessary occurrence does not imply necessary conceptualization, thus blocking al-Razi's proof for the necessity of absolute knowledge.

Knowledge of Existence Does Not Imply Conceptualization of Absolute Knowledge
Author presents an objection and two responses about knowledge and conceptualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the necessity of knowing one's own existence does not necessitate conceptualizing absolute knowledge, and the two responses to this objection.

Core Idea
Knowing something is not the same as conceptualizing that knowledge.

This passage addresses an objection: if knowledge of one's own existence is necessary, then absolute knowledge (the universal) must also be necessary because the particular falls under the universal. The author provides two responses. First, the occurrence of particular knowledge is not its conceptualization; we often have knowledge without being aware of its nature. Second, the entailment would only hold if knowledge were essential to the particular and if some particular were intuitively known by essence—both denied.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening line of the passage which states the objection: that necessary knowledge of existence entails absolute knowledge. Note how the objector reasons from particular to universal.
The objection is implicit in the phrase 'المطلوب والجواب عنه أن الضروري حصول علم جزئي'.
2
Study the first response
Focus on the phrase 'وهو غير تصوره وغير مستلزم له'. This is the core of the first response: the occurrence of knowledge is not its conceptualization and does not necessitate it. The author gives an example: we often have particular knowledges without conceptualizing them.
The distinction between having knowledge and conceptualizing it is crucial.
3
Study the second response
The second response begins with 'ويجوز أن يجاب عنه أيضاً'. It argues that the objection's entailment only works if knowledge is essential (dhātī) to what is under it and if some individual is intuitively known by essence (bi-al-kunn badīhatan). Both are denied.
This response tackles the premises of the objection rather than the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المطلوب (والجواب ) عنه ( أن الضروري حصول علم جزئي ( متعلق بوجوده ) فإن هذا العلم حاصل لكل أحد بلا نظر (وهو) أي حصول ذلك العلم الجزئي ( غير تصوره وغير مستلزم (له إذ كثيراً ما تحصل لنا علوم جزئية بمعلومات مخصوصة، ولا نتصور تلك العلوم مع كونها حاصلة لنا، بل نحتاج في تصورها إلى توجه مستأنف إليها، فلا يكون حصولها عين تصورها ولا مستلزماً له ، وإذا لم يكن ذلك العلم الجزئي المتعلق بوجوده متصوراً ( فلا يلزم تصور العلم المطلق أصلاً ( فضلاً عن أن يكون ) تصوره ( ضرورياً)، ويجوز أن يجاب عنه أن يجاب عنه أيضاً بأنه إنما يتم إذا كان العلم ذاتياً لما تحته، وكان شيء من أفراده متصوراً بالكنه بديهةً وكلاهما ممنوعان
The desired [objection] and the answer to it: that the necessary thing is the occurrence of a particular knowledge (ʿilm juzʾī) related to His existence, for this knowledge is obtained by everyone without reflection (naẓar). And it—i.e., the occurrence of that particular knowledge—is not its conceptualization (taṣawwur) nor does it necessitate it, for often particular knowledges of specific known things occur to us, but we do not conceptualize those knowledges while they are present to us; rather, we need in their conceptualization a renewed attention to them. So their occurrence is neither identical to their conceptualization nor does it necessitate it. And when that particular knowledge related to His existence is not conceptualized, then conceptualization of absolute knowledge (al-ʿilm al-muṭlaq) is not entailed at all, let alone that its conceptualization be necessary. And it is also possible to answer it [the objection] by saying that it [the argument] only holds if knowledge is essential (dhātī) to what is under it, and if some of its individuals are conceptualized by essence (bi-al-kunn) intuitively (badīhatan)—both of which are denied.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and two responses. The objector claims that since knowledge of one's existence is necessary, absolute knowledge must also be necessary because the particular is under the universal. The first response argues that the occurrence of that particular knowledge is not its conceptualization; we often have knowledge without being aware of its nature, so no conceptualization of the particular occurs, and thus no conceptualization of the universal is entailed. The second response argues that the entailment would only hold if the universal were essential to the particular and if some particular were intuitively known by essence—both conditions are denied.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the occurrence of knowledge (ḥuṣūl) is distinct from its conceptualization (taṣawwur). We often have knowledge without conceptualizing it; conceptualization requires renewed attention.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Knowledge as a Component of Assent: Objection and Refutation
Objection that knowledge is a component of the assent, and answer refutes the necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that knowledge is a component of a necessary assent fails to prove that its conceptualization is necessary, and why the distinction between occurrence and conceptualization is crucial.

Core Idea
Necessary occurrence of knowledge does not mean necessary conceptualization.

Having established the framework of assent and its components, an objector pushes further: if everyone knows their own existence and knows that they know it, then knowledge itself becomes part of that assent—so its conceptualization should be intuitive. This challenge requires careful unpacking. The answer clarifies a subtle but vital distinction between the occurrence of knowledge and its conceptualization, showing that the objection rests on a false equivalence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector claims that since everyone knows that they exist, they also know that they know—so knowledge is a component of the assent. This would make the conceptualization of knowledge prior to the assent, and thus itself intuitive.
Notice that the objector is trying to force the conceptualization of absolute knowledge to be intuitive.
2
Identify the key distinction in the answer
The answer first points out that occurrence of knowledge is not the same as its conceptualization. Then it shows that the phrase 'knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations' is ambiguous—it could refer to knowledge as subject or predicate, so it cannot force necessity of absolute knowledge's conceptualization.
This distinction between occurrence and conceptualization is crucial.
3
Reflect on why the objection fails
Even if knowledge of knowledge is necessary after attention, that necessity applies to the occurrence, not to the conceptualization. The objection conflates these two, and the ambiguity of 'one of the two' prevents any forced conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال :) لا نقتصر على ما ذكر بل نقول : إن كل واحد يعلم بالضرورة أنه موجود، ( ويعلم ) أيضاً كذلك ( أنه يعلم بذلك والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق)، وهو أيضاً، فيكون تصوره السابق على التصديق البديهي أولى أن يكون بديهياً نحن بديهي ( : قوله : فلا يكون حصولها عين (تصورها وما قيل من أن العلم بالعلم بعد الالتفات إليه ضروري على ما نص المصنف عليه في الأعراض، فيكون العلم بهذا العلم ضرورياً، والعلم المطلق جزء منه، فيتم التقريب فليس بشيء لأن معنى ذلك أن العلم بحصول العلم بعد الالتفات ضروري لا أن تصوره ضروري حتى يلزم ضرورية المطلق. لأنه قوله : ( والعلم أحد تصوري هذا التصديق) إنما قال : أحد التصورين من غير تعيين يجوز أن يجعل موضوعاً بأن يقال : العلم بهذا العلم ضروري، وأن يجعل محمولاً بأن يقال : كل أحد عالم بهذا العلم بالضرورة، وبهذا ظهر أنه لا يجوز أن يقال أحد التصورات الثلاثة، فمن قال : إنما لم يقل أحد التصورات اتباعاً للمتقدمين، وإن اعتبار تصور النسبة في التصديق من تدقيقات المتأخرين، فقد ركب شططاً .
It may not be said: We do not restrict to what was mentioned, but we say: Every person knows necessarily that he exists, and also knows likewise that he knows that, and knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations (taṣawwur) of this assent (taṣdīq), and it also [is a conceptualization]; so its conceptualization preceding the intuitive assent is more fitting to be intuitive. We [respond] intuitive. His saying: So its occurrence is not identical to its conceptualization. And what has been said that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary as the author stated in al-Aʿrāḍ, so knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, and absolute knowledge is part of it, so the argument is complete—it is nothing, because the meaning of that is that knowledge of the occurrence of knowledge after attention is necessary, not that its conceptualization is necessary such that the necessity of the absolute follows. Because his saying: (And knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations of this assent) he only said one of the two conceptualizations without specification, so it is permissible to make it subject, saying: knowledge of this knowledge is necessary, or to make it predicate, saying: everyone knows this knowledge necessarily. And by this it appears that it is not permissible to say one of the three conceptualizations. So whoever said that he did not say one of the conceptualizations following the earlier scholars, and that considering the conceptualization of the relation in assent is from the investigations of the later scholars—he has gone far astray.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer. The objector argues that since everyone knows that they exist, they also know that they know this—so knowledge is a component of that assent, and thus its conceptualization (being prior to the intuitive assent) should be intuitive as well. The answer first notes that the occurrence of knowledge is not its conceptualization; the claim that knowledge of knowledge after attention is necessary refers to the occurrence, not the conceptualization. Then it points out that the phrase 'knowledge is one of the two conceptualizations' does not specify which part (subject or predicate) it is, so it cannot be used to argue that the conceptualization of absolute knowledge is necessary. This refutes the attempt to force a regress.

Intuitive Assent Does Not Necessitate Intuitive Conceptualizations
Author refutes the claim that an intuitive assent implies its components are intuitive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the intuitiveness of an assent does not require its conceptual components to be intuitive, and how the author defends this position against objections.

Core Idea
Intuitive assent can exist even if its component concepts are acquired.

This passage presents a critical refinement in Islamic epistemology: the distinction between the intuitiveness of a judgment and the intuitiveness of its conceptual components. The author refutes the claim that an intuitive assent must have intuitive parts, using the example of children and simpletons to show that assent can be intuitive without any acquisition. Let's read the source to see how he dismantles the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the objection, response, and further clarifications.
The passage includes many parenthetical glosses; focus on the main argument flow.
2
Identify the main objection
The objector claims that an intuitive assent could have all acquired conceptualizations, so intuitiveness of assent does not imply intuitiveness of its components.
This objection is presented as 'فإن قلت'.
3
Follow the refutation
The author responds by stating that this particular assent is free from reflection entirely, not just in judgment but also in its ends, as even simpletons have it.
The author uses the example of simple-minded and children to show the assent is intuitive without any acquisition.
4
Understand the terminological dispute
The author dismisses the dispute about whether assent is solely the judgment or includes conceptualizations, because the assent under discussion is absolutely free from reflection.
This shows that the intuition of the assent is independent of the definitional debate.
5
Address the circularity concern
A potential circularity is resolved: the claim is for the intuitiveness of a specific part (knowledge) in detail, while the evidence is the intuitiveness of the whole assent in summary, so no circularity.
The author mentions an analog in the discussion of existence.

حجر، بل ومع الجهل بحقيقة الحيز قوله : ( في جواب هذا التقرير خصه بهذا التقرير، وإن صح كونه جواباً على التقرير الأول لأنه تخلل بين التقرير الثاني، وجوابه لأنا نقول : إلخ وإنما صح كونه جواباً على التقرير الأول، لأنه وقع فيه العلم الخاص، أعني العلم بكونه موجوداً موضوعاً، وحكم عليه بأنه ضروري ليمكن أن يقال : لا يلزم من كون العلم بأنه موجود ضرورياً بداهة العلم الذي وقع فيه موضوعاً، لأنه تصديق ضروري ولا يلزم من بداهته بداهة أطرافه فتدبر. قوله : ( إذ لا تخلو إلخ) أشار بهذا الدليل إلى أن المثبت بداهة التصديق مع قطع النظر عن خصوصية الأطراف، والمثبت ببداهة التصديق بداهة العلم الذي هو أحد طرفيه بخصوصه فلا دور . قوله : ( مستغن عن النظر مطلقاً) أي باعتبار الحكم والطرفين سواء كانا جزأين أو شرطين . قوله: (ولا) في شيء من أطرافه لا يقال : فحينئذ يلزم المصادرة لأن أحد طرفيه هو العلم الذي يراد إثبات بداهة تصوره لأنا نقول : المدعي بداهة جزء معين تفصيلاً أعني العلم، والدليل بداهة هذا التصديق بجميع أجزائه إجمالاً، فلا مصادرة لاختلاف العنوان، ونظيره كبرى القياس سيجيء في بحث الوجود . مع نتيجته كما قوله : ( ولا يحتاج فيه إلى تصورهما بالكنه ولو سلم فإنما يتم لو كان المطلق ذاتياً للعلم الجزئي كما ذكره الشارح فيما مر ، نعم لو استدل بأن كل أحد يعلم أن له علماً مطلقاً تعين جواب المصنف .
For if you say in answer to this exposition: from the intuitiveness (badāhah) of the assent, the intuitiveness of its two conceptualizations does not follow, nor the intuitiveness of anything from them; for the intuitive assent is that which does not depend, after conceptualization of the two ends, on reflection. So it is possible that all its conceptualizations are acquired (kasbiyya), and thus the inference from the intuitiveness of the assent to the intuitiveness of any of its conceptualizations is not valid at all. I say in refutation of this answer: the claim is the occurrence of this assent without reflection in the judgment nor in anything from its ends, for the simple-minded and children are not free from it—those who cannot engage in acquisition, neither in judgment nor in conceptualization. And the dispute about terminology—that assent is only the judgment alone and the conceptualizations of its ends are conditions external to it, so the intuitive part is the judgment independent of inference even if its conceptualizations are theoretical, and assent is not an expression of the composite whole consisting of the judgment and the conceptualizations of its ends such that its intuitiveness would necessitate the intuitiveness of its conceptualizations—does not avail in this context, because you know that this assent under discussion is absolutely free from reflection. Then he began a response to 'it may not be said' with his saying: 'For we say: it suffices in assent the conceptualization of the two ends in some manner, and there is no need to conceptualize them by essence (bi-al-kunn),' just as we judge about a specific body seen from a distance that it occupies a specific place, while being ignorant of its true nature—whether it is a human or a stone—indeed even while being ignorant of the true nature of space. His saying: (In answer to this exposition) he specified it with this exposition, although it is valid as an answer to the first exposition because it intervened between the second exposition and its answer 'for we say' etc. It is valid as an answer to the first exposition only because in it occurred the particular knowledge, namely the knowledge that he exists as subject, and it was ruled necessary so that it could be said: from the necessity of the knowledge that he exists, the intuitiveness of the knowledge that occurred as subject does not follow, because it is a necessary assent and from its intuitiveness the intuitiveness of its ends does not follow—so reflect. His saying: (For the simple-minded are not free etc.) He pointed by this proof to the fact that the proponent of the intuitiveness of the assent disregards the specificity of the ends, and the proponent of the intuitiveness of the assent [as proof] for the intuitiveness of the knowledge that is one of its ends in particular—so there is no circularity. His saying: (Absolutely free from reflection) i.e., with respect to the judgment and the two ends, whether they are parts or conditions. His saying: (Nor) in anything of its ends—it may not be said: then there follows circularity because one of its ends is the knowledge whose conceptualization is intended to be proven intuitive; for we say: the claimant claims the intuitiveness of a specific part in detail, namely knowledge, and the evidence is the intuitiveness of this assent with all its parts in sum, so there is no circularity due to the difference of aspect. Its analog will come in the discussion of existence with its conclusion. His saying: (And there is no need to conceptualize them by essence) and even if it is granted, it would only hold if the universal were essential (dhātī) to the particular knowledge as the commentator mentioned earlier. Yes, if one argued that everyone knows that he has absolute knowledge, then the author's answer would be determined.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of an objection that the intuitiveness of an assent does not imply the intuitiveness of its conceptual components. The author first presents the objection: an intuitive assent is defined as not depending on reflection after conceptualizing the two ends, so its conceptualizations could all be acquired; thus one cannot infer intuitiveness of any conceptualization from the intuitiveness of the assent. The response argues that the claim is that this specific assent occurs without any reflection at all, not just in judgment but also in the ends, as even simpletons and children have it while incapable of acquisition. The terminological dispute about assent being solely the judgment, with conceptualizations as external conditions, is dismissed because this assent is free from reflection absolutely. Then a further response addresses a potential objection about circularity: the claim is for the intuitiveness of a specific part (knowledge) in detail, while the evidence is the intuitiveness of the whole assent in summary, so no circularity. Lastly, it notes that even if one concedes that conceptualization by essence is not needed, the argument for absolute knowledge would only hold if the universal were essential to the particular, which is not granted. An alternative proof is mentioned.
